World News: World Watch
530 words
8 November 2013
The Wall Street Journal
J
A12
English
Copyright 2013 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
CHINA
Police Detain Local Man
Over Shanxi Bombings
Chinese authorities have detained a local man over explosions in the northern Chinese city of Taiyuan this
week that killed one person, state media reported.
The official Xinhua News Agency said Friday that authorities had detained Feng Zhijun, a 41-year-old
ex-convict and resident of Taiyuan. Xinhua , citing police, said he had admitted to the crime.
The explosions on Wednesday took place in front of the Communist Party headquarters for Shanxi province.
Authorities attributed the explosions to homemade bombs, after finding steel balls and circuit boards
scattered around the scene.
-- Carlos Tejada
---
CONGO
Nearly 2,000 M23 Rebels
Surrender in Uganda
At least 1,700 former fighters of the biggest rebel group in the Democratic Republic of the Congo and their
lead commander have surrendered to the Ugandan military after Congo government forces drove them from
their remaining strongholds near the Ugandan border this week, ending a nearly two-year rebellion, Ugandan
military officials said.
Most of the surrendering fighters in the group known as M23 crossed into Uganda Thursday, days after they
were routed by Congolese troops backed by a United Nations combat brigade in North Kivu, a tin- and
gold-producing province in Congo's east, Ugandan military spokesman Lt. Col. Paddy Ankunda said.
More than two million people have fled their homes within Congo since fighting broke out between the
government and the rebels in early 2012, aid agencies estimate, and many refugees have crossed into
Uganda and Rwanda.
-- Nicholas Bariyo
---
BRITAIN
Officials Say Snowden Leaks
Damaged Terror Fight
Britain's most senior intelligence officials said the leaks by fugitive former U.S. intelligence operative Edward
Snowden have been extremely damaging to British information-gathering operations, including in monitoring
terrorists.
Iain Lobban, the head of U.K. Government Communications Headquarters, known as GCHQ, told a panel of
politicians here that his agency's work depends on intelligence targets being unaware or uncertain of the

--- Page 6 ---
Page 6 of 192 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
methods used, and of their success. He said that as a direct result of media reports about the leaked material
in recent months, his agency has seen near-daily discussion of the revelations in specific terms among some
intelligence targets -- including terrorist groups in the Middle East and South Asia.
"We have actually seen chat around specific terrorist groups, including closer to home, discussing how to
avoid what they now perceive to be vulnerable communications methods," Mr. Lobban said.
-- Cassell Bryan-Low
---
VENEZUELA
Inflation Rises Above 50%
For First Time Since 1999
Venezuela's economy hit a dubious milestone Thursday: Inflation is now above 50% a year for the first time
since the late President Hugo Chavez took power in 1999.
The country's central bank said prices rose 5.1% in October, pushing the 12-month inflation rate to 54.3% --
the highest mark since the country was hit by hyperinflation in the mid-1990s.
Two factors have triggered the surge. The first is ramped-up public spending from President Nicolas Maduro,
Mr. Chavez's successor. At the same time, currency controls Mr. Chavez implemented in 2003 have made
access to dollars difficult, causing widespread shortages.
-- Ezequiel Minaya

Nash-less Rangers Shut Down Crosby
Sports
Nash-less Rangers Shut Down Crosby
By Dave Caldwell
587 words
7 November 2013
The Wall Street Journal
J
A25
English
Copyright 2013 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
PIT 1
NYR 5
The transformed Madison Square Garden received an esteemed visitor Wednesday: Sidney Crosby, the
26-year-old Pittsburgh Penguins ' center and, once again, the scourge of the National Hockey League , with
more points than any other player.
But on Wednesday, Brian Boyle, the Rangers' inelegant center, outscored Crosby, flipping in his first goal of
the season and adding an assist as the Rangers snapped the Penguins' four-game winning streak with a 5-1
rout.
That Crosby, held without a point Wednesday, is as good as ever, however, might be positive news for the
Rangers, too.
Nearly three years have passed since Crosby suffered a concussion that kept him out of action for almost a
year, but he is as creative and effective as he was before Washington forward David Steckel blindsided him
on Jan. 1, 2011.
"I don't really think there's really anything I've changed," Crosby said Wednesday morning.
Crosby's self-evaluation may carry more clout here because the Rangers' elite high-scoring forward, Rick
Nash, has what the team said is a "head injury" that is suspected to be a concussion.
Nash, who was hit in the head by San Jose defenseman Brad Stuart on Oct. 8, missed his 10th game
Wednesday and is out indefinitely.
A Rangers' spokesman replied, "No. Sorry," when asked if he had an update.
Crosby has learned a lot about concussions since Steckel hit him, and even though Rangers fans don't like
him very much, he had enough of a sense of compassion to send a text message to Nash, with whom he won
a gold medal at the 2010 Winter Olympics for Canada.
"Nothing too deep," Crosby said. "I just wanted to see how he was doing."
The Rangers' lack of progress reports on Nash has fed speculation that the team make a trade for a forward,
or forwards, to replace him. First-year coach Alain Vigneault sidestepped a question about potential trades
Wednesday.
"We need to get the pieces we have here to play better," he said.
The Rangers are now 7-8-0 -- not that bad, considering they played their first nine games on the road. After
Thursday's game in Columbus, they'll play only two games in the following eight days, practically a vacation.
Pittsburgh won 11 of its first 16 games this season, limiting opponents' shots on goal and, more important to
coach Dan Bylsma, scoring chances. "It's more of a mind-set than anything systematically," he said.
Crosby, the captain, sounds as if he has bought in. He said the Penguins could do a better job keeping the
puck in their opponents' zone, but he also said, "When you put pressure on us, we've been in just the right
spots, and not panicking."
Crosby has only eight goals in the Penguins' first 16 games -- slightly below his career average of a goal
every 1.98 games.

--- Page 8 ---
Page 8 of 192 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
The Penguins have won the Stanley Cup only once with Crosby, nearly five years ago, and a lot has
happened to him since then, including the head injury that nearly ended his career. Now he is back in form,
an encouraging sign for more than just his team.
When asked how he judged his recovery from his concussion, Crosby said, "It's based on symptoms and
things like that, so in that way, you have to stay patient."
His patience, anyway, is being rewarded three years later.

Shares Get Push From Fed
Wednesday's Markets
Shares Get Push From Fed
By Alexandra Scaggs
540 words
7 November 2013
The Wall Street Journal
J
C4
English
Copyright 2013 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Stocks rose, with the Dow industrials setting another record, on increasing investor hopes for continued
easy-money policies from the Federal Reserve.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average advanced 128.66 points, or 0.8%, to close at a record of 15746.88. The
blue-chip stock average has hit 33 all-time highs so far this year.
The S&P 500 climbed 7.52 points, or 0.4%, to 1770.49. It is up 24% this year to date. The Nasdaq Composite
Index slipped 7.92 points, or 0.2%, to 3931.95, as declines in biotechnology stocks weighed on the index.
Better-than-expected economic data from Europe helped support markets in early trading. But hopes for
continuing stimulus efforts from the Fed drove much of the day's advance, sparking gains in
interest-rate-sensitive stock sectors and Treasury prices. In recent months, markets have swung alongside
investor expectations for the central bank's timeline for trimming, or "tapering," its bond-buying program.
"On a day like today, when people think the taper may take longer, stocks rally," said Paul Karos, senior
portfolio manager for Whitebox Advisors, which manages $2.6 billion. But he hasn't changed his outlook, he
said, adding markets owed their Wednesday moves to "mainly the ebb and flow of speculation."
Utilities, telecommunications and consumer-staples sectors led gainers in the S&P 500. Those sectors often
trade as proxies for the bond market, and sold off sharply over the summer after the Fed said it would
consider paring back its bond-buying program.
Mr. Karos said his firm has been cautious about buying stocks in those sectors, and making sure to choose
investments that will hold up when Treasury yields rise.
In Europe, stocks gained after better-than-expected economic data and as expectations remained that the
European Central Bank may act to bolster the economy, possibly as soon as Thursday's policy decision.
"Investors will be watching the ECB announcement closely in light of last week's euro-zone inflation report,
which was worryingly low," said Stephen Macklow-Smith , a European equities portfolio manager at J.P.
Morgan Asset Management , which oversees $1.5 trillion of assets. "It's possible the ECB will decide to trim
interest rates at the margin."
But Friday's closely watched October employment report will take top billing this week, as investors try to
determine when the Fed could start paring its easy-money policies. Central-bank officials have said they will
pay close attention to the labor market to determine when the economy is strong enough to weather a paring
of stimulus efforts.
Crude-oil futures rose 1.5% to settle at $94.80 a barrel, after settling at a five-month low on Tuesday.
Gold futures gained 0.7% to settle at $1,317.70 a troy ounce. The dollar edged higher against the yen but
eased against the euro.
In Asia early Thursday, stock markets were moderately lower. Japan's Nikkei stock average was down 0.5%,
Hong Kong's Hang Seng index was down 0.6%, Australia's S&P/ASX 200 was down 0.3% and South Korea's
Kospi was down 0.2%.

Race to Mine Bitcoin Gathers Steam --- Rush to Generate Valuable Virtual Currency Fuels Litigation, Regulatory Scrutiny and a Quest for...
Race to Mine Bitcoin Gathers Steam --- Rush to Generate Valuable Virtual Currency Fuels Litigation,
Regulatory Scrutiny and a Quest for Speed
By Robin Sidel
829 words
6 November 2013
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2013 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
The price of bitcoin isn't the only thing about the virtual currency that is going through the roof.
Mining costs, industry parlance for the investments in sophisticated technology required to create bitcoin, are
soaring as companies and people race to build ever-more-powerful computers to jump into the market.
In the mining process, bitcoin enthusiasts, technology experts and fledgling firms essentially generate units of
the currency by solving complicated math problems with their computers.
The growing popularity and rising prices of bitcoin are causing a mad dash for the powerful computing power
needed to generate the virtual currency, leading to profit pressure for companies and individual enthusiasts
working to create their own stashes.
The speed at which such operations take place is now 40 times faster than in January, according to the
Genesis Block, a New York research and data firm that tracks the bitcoin industry. In addition, hardware
companies are hustling to keep up with orders for new equipment.
"The dollars that have been invested in this space are being reinvested into computational power," says Greg
Schvey, head of research at the company.
There is a finite supply of 21 million bitcoin that can be created. There are roughly 12 million currently in
existence.
The furor surrounding bitcoin production was referenced last week in a lawsuit filed against two bitcoin
companies in which a New York-based investment firm contends bitcoin startup CoinLab Inc. and its mining
unit violated a bitcoin-production contract.
The suit, filed by Bitvestment Partners LLC, contends that CoinLab reneged on a pact to produce nearly
8,000 bitcoin.
The lawsuit was a contributing factor in last week's bankruptcy filing of the CoinLab mining unit, Alydian Inc.
"Due to the lawsuit and the dramatic increase in mining speed in the Bitcoin network (lowering returns for all
Bitcoin miners, including Alydian), Alydian felt that its duty was to ensure that Alydian's customers, creditors
and investors maximize their possible returns through Chapter 11 bankruptcy protection," Peter Vessenes,
CoinLab's chief executive, said in an emailed statement Tuesday. He said the lawsuit was baseless.
A lawyer representing Bitvestment declined to comment on Mr. Vessenes's statement. The lawsuit was filed
in U.S. district court for the New York's Southern district.
The lawsuit and bankruptcy are the latest scramble around bitcoin, a fast-growing virtual currency that has
seen roller-coaster trading and fueled a modern-day gold rush.
Bitcoin's increased popularity also has brought more scrutiny. A pair of Senate committees will hold hearings
on the policy issues raised by virtual currencies in the coming weeks, according to Senate aides, a
development that comes amid growing attention from government regulators on digital forms of money.
Bitcoin is a four-year-old virtual currency that isn't backed by a central bank and can be traded on a number
of exchanges or swapped privately. A growing number of merchants also accept bitcoin as payment for
goods and services, because the transaction costs associated with the currency are generally cheaper than
those with credit cards or debit cards.

--- Page 11 ---
Page 11 of 192 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Bitcoin traded at record highs Tuesday, rising to $252.61 on the Tokyo-based Mt. Gox exchange. That gives
those 8,000 disputed bitcoin a value of roughly $2 million.
But the rising prices have brought an increased push by so-called miners to use the speediest computers to
get more bitcoin.
"There is an arms race for customized hardware that is more and more powerful and can process
transactions faster and faster. But it is making it difficult to get a return on investment," says Jeremy Allaire , a
technology entrepreneur who last week announced a new bitcoin-related company that received $9 million in
venture-capital funding.
The power and speed at which the computers attempt to solve the problems and accumulate more bitcoin is
measured in the so-called hashrate. Recently, the measure has soared and is now tracked in "petahash,"
bitcoin experts say.
Alydian's bankruptcy filing surprised many observers, because CoinLab is one of the best-known names in
the virtual-currency industry, describing itself as a "bitcoin business incubator." Silicon Valley's venture-capital
firm Draper Associates pumped $500,000 into CoinLab last year.
Mr. Vessenes also is the chairman of the Bitcoin Foundation, a nonprofit trade group that promotes the use of
bitcoin.
The process of producing bitcoin is being further complicated by arrangements in which some mining
companies essentially agreed to sell bitcoin to investors in exchange for cash to fund equipment needs,
according to people who track the industry. With bitcoin prices rising, it is now more expensive for the miners
to deliver that currency to their investors.
"Mining has gotten hypercompetitive very quickly," says Alex Ferrara , a partner at Bessemer Venture
Partners , who is assessing investment opportunities in the bitcoin industry.

Stocks Inch Up Amid Mixed Signals
Tuesday's Markets
Stocks Inch Up Amid Mixed Signals
By Tomi Kilgore
632 words
5 November 2013
The Wall Street Journal
J
C4
English
Copyright 2013 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Stocks edged broadly higher with investors weighing lackluster factory-orders data against encouraging
earnings results following the market's recent gains.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average gained 23.57 points, or 0.2%, to 15639.12. The S&P 500 index added
6.29 points, or 0.4%, to 1767.93, and the Nasdaq Composite Index rose 14.55 points, or 0.4%, to 3936.59.
All 10 of the S&P 500 sectors gained ground, led by strength in energy and telecom stocks.
The Dow briefly slid into negative territory early in the session before recovering midday, following data
showing factory orders for September increased 1.7% on the month, just shy of expectations of a 1.8% rise.
For August, factory orders declined 0.1% versus forecasts for growth of 0.3%. The August reading was
delayed due to the government shutdown.
The S&P 500, which has gained 24% in the year to date, was just below the all-time closing high of 1771.95
seen early last week.
The yield on the 10-year Treasury note edged down to 2.602% from 2.618% late Friday.
Michael Strauss , chief investment strategist at Commonfund , which oversees about $24 billion in assets,
isn't ruling out bouts of weakness heading into year-end, considering the strength seen so far this year. But
he believes any pullback will remain relatively shallow, as earnings have held up fairly well and are providing
some "underlying support" for stocks.
"The trend that we've been in is probably still your friend," Mr. Strauss said. He said that while the market
may not rise at the same accelerated pace of the past year, he remains bullish on equities.
With 75% of the S&P 500 companies having reported third-quarter results as of early Monday, earnings are
on pace of year-over-year growth of 3.1%, according to FactSet.
That is slightly above expectations of 3% earnings growth before the start of earnings-reporting season.
"It's progress, not perfection," said John Stoltzfus , chief market strategist at Oppenheimer Asset
Management. "Earnings have given some justification for the market's recent moves."
Some coming concerns investors may face include a potential reduction in monetary stimulus by the Federal
Reserve, with some anticipating a move as early as year-end.
Many believe the Fed's highly accommodative policies, particularly the $85 billion-a-month bond buying
program, have helped power the market's rally to record highs.
In corporate news, BlackBerry tumbled $1.28, or 16%, to $6.50 on heavy volume after the company
abandoned its plans to be taken private, saying instead that it raised $1 billion through the issuance of
convertible debt. In addition, Thorsten Heins will step down as chief executive and resign from the board of
directors.
Twitter increased the price range of its expected initial public offering of 70 million shares later this week to
$23 to $25 a share from $17 to $20 a share, bringing the offering's value to $1.75 billion.
December crude-oil futures settled up less than 0.1% at $94.62 a barrel to snap a four-session losing streak,
while November gold futures gained 0.1% to $1,314.60 an ounce. The dollar inched higher against the yen
and fell slightly against the euro.

--- Page 13 ---
Page 13 of 192 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
European stocks rose, with the Stoxx Europe 600 climbing 0.3% to the highest close since May 2008.
In Asia early Tuesday, Japan's Nikkei was down 0.1% after disappointing earnings from Nissan; Australia's
S&P/ASX 200 was up 0.8% ahead of a rate decision by the country's central bank later in the day; and
South Korea's Kospi was down 0.4%.

Corrections & Amplifications
Corrections & Amplifications
Corrections & Amplifications
495 words
5 November 2013
The Wall Street Journal
J
A2
English
Copyright 2013 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
The expiration of a temporary boost in food-stamp benefits would leave 48 million Americans with an
estimated $11 billion less to spend over the next three years, according to the Center on Budget and Policy
Priorities . A Marketplace article on Thursday about the expiration incorrectly put the total at $16 billion.
(See: "Retailers Brace For Reduced Food Stamps" -- WSJ October 31, 2013)
---
Expectations of a cut in interest rates by the European Central Bank rose Friday after an anemic euro-zone
inflation report. The headline of an In the Markets article on Saturday about the euro incorrectly referred to
the prospect of a rate increase.
(See: "Currency Trading: Euro Skids From 2-Year High -- Worries of Rate Increase by Central Bank Rise
After Anemic Inflation Report" -- WSJ November 2, 2013)
---
Best Buy Co .'s sales slipped in the company's second quarter. The retailer hasn't yet reported results for its
third quarter. A Marketplace article on Monday about "showrooming" incorrectly said Best Buy 's sales fell in
the third quarter.
(See: "Fear of 'Showrooming' Fades -- Best Buy , Other Retailers Optimistic That Price-Matching Can Stanch

WEEKEND INVESTOR --- What's Next for Gold? --- After stealing the spotlight during the financial crisis, the precious metal has lost its...
WEEKEND INVESTOR --- What's Next for Gold? --- After stealing the spotlight during the financial
crisis, the precious metal has lost its luster as stocks have regained center stage; Is it still a
worthwhile investment? We investigate
By Joe Light
1,946 words
2 November 2013
The Wall Street Journal
J
B7
English
Copyright 2013 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
To its fans, gold is an inflation-fighting, economy-hedging, dollar-busting superhero asset class.
Lately, it has just been a dud.
The precious metal this year is on track to record its first yearly fall since the year 2000. On Friday, it closed
at $1,313.10 an ounce, down 22% for 2013.
According to the International Monetary Fund , global central banks, which had recently been big gold
buyers, in the past year have reduced their purchases.
So should investors throw gold on the scrapheap?
A small allocation to gold won't kill an investor's portfolio, but experts say you should think twice before
leaning on it heavily to hedge against inflation, economic collapse or any other specific fear. Most stated
goals of gold investors can be accomplished with other, potentially cheaper, asset classes.
The performance of other assets during gold's recent slump just makes the sting worse. Large U.S. stocks
are up big time so far this year, with the S&P 500 returning 25%, including dividends, through Thursday.
Small-company stocks, as measured by the Russell 2000, have done even better, returning 31%.
Even U.S. bonds, which are vulnerable to the threat of rising interest rates, have lost only 1.1%, as
measured by the Barclays U.S. Aggregate Bond Index.
That has left gold nearly alone in its misery.
John Pierce, an 80-year-old retiree in Haddonfield, N.J., bought a few thousand dollars' worth of American
Eagle gold coins in November 2011, hoping they would protect him from inflation and potential disruptions
from continuing squabbles in Washington over the debt ceiling and spending cuts.
Mr. Pierce, who has belonged to stock investment clubs for a quarter-century, had never before invested in
gold.
"I heard all the stories about how gold has gone up in the last 10 years," he says. "I thought it was time to get
my feet wet."
Since then, Mr. Pierce's investment is down about 20%.
Adding to the anguish, the concerns that drove many investors toward gold have been validated.
Since Mr. Pierce bought gold at $1,700 an ounce, Washington gridlock has led to severe spending cuts, a
debt-ceiling debacle and a government shutdown.
The only thing Mr. Pierce -- and many of his fellow investors -- got wrong was how gold would react.
For decades, gold's behavior has defied attempts to define it. At times, investors have said they hold it to
protect against inflation, a poor economy, and a decline in the U.S. dollar.
Depending on the time period, each argument has been upended, says Michael Cuggino , president and
portfolio manager of the Permanent Portfolio Family of Funds. The company's $11 billion flagship mutual
fund, Permanent Portfolio, keeps about 20% of its holdings in gold and charges annual expenses of 0.69%,
or $69 per $10,000 invested.

--- Page 17 ---
Page 17 of 192 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Instead, gold seems to play different roles at different times, Mr. Cuggino says.
In the early 2000s, gold acted like other commodities, such as copper and oil, and rose amid demand from
fast-rising emerging markets and a weak dollar, among other factors. In 2008, gold provided a haven when
other risky assets plummeted. The metal is still up 68% since Lehman Brothers Holdings collapsed on Sept.
15 of that year.
Now, Mr. Cuggino says, it is acting as a hedge against potential interest rate rises, slowing economic growth
and other uncertainties.
"Gold is somewhat of a chameleon of an asset because investors tend to value it as a function of something
else, and it's sometimes tough to tell what that is," Mr. Cuggino says.
That is partly because no one knows how to value gold. Unlike stocks, which have dividends and earnings
growth, and bonds, which pay interest, gold doesn't generate cash flows.
That makes the shiny stuff quite literally worth only what the next investor will pay for it. Even more so than
stocks, gold's price is driven by investors' perceptions of its allure and scarcity.
Gold's remarkable run since the turn of the century brought lots of conservative, investors into the asset class
who never considered it before. Between the end of the year 2000, when gold closed at $272 an ounce, and
August 2011, the precious metal's price increased nearly sevenfold.
One driver: the advent of gold exchange-traded funds, such as State Street Global Advisors' SPDR Gold
Shares , which made its debut in 2004, and BlackRock's iShares Gold Trust , which launched the following
year. The ETFs, which charge annual expenses of 0.4% and 0.25%, respectively, trade throughout the day
like stocks, which makes it easier for small investors to move into and out of gold quickly and cheaply.
Since its debut, SPDR Gold Shares , the larger of the two, has collected nearly $16 billion, which, when
combined with price gains, has brought the fund's total assets to about $37 billion, according to research firm
IndexUniverse.
Gold's rise led even so-called value investors, who typically rely on earnings fundamentals to pick stocks, to
dip their toes in the water.
In his main portfolio, William Peterson a 65-year-old retired information-technology professional in Phoenix,
picks stocks based on standard metrics such as price/earnings ratios and earnings-growth projections.
But in 2003, as the dot-com bubble finished deflating, he and his wife bought more than $1,000 worth of
collectible gold coins.
"The price was going up relatively rapidly, so we thought we ought to be in that area," he says. "It felt like I
was protecting myself from another downturn in stocks."
Asset managers also jumped on board.
In its 22-year history, Leuthold Weeden Capital Management in Minneapolis had never held gold in a mutual
fund or separate account. But after a decadelong run that saw gold's price more than triple to about $900, the
firm decided to work the asset class into its traditional mix of stocks and bonds.
Some clients were relieved.
"We had a client base that seemed to say, 'These guys are finally getting it,'" says Doug Ramsey , the chief
investment officer at Leuthold, which manages $1.6 billion.
The firm has since backtracked. At one point, it held as much as 7% of its typical portfolios in gold, but now
its allocation is merely 1%.
Professional money managers disagree over the cause for gold's recent drop.
Eric Sprott , CEO of Toronto-based Sprott Asset Management, says gold's price has recently dropped
because faulty statistics underreport demand and overestimate supply.
In October he wrote an open letter to an industry group, asking them to change the way they calculate supply
and demand for gold in a way that would make the metal seem like a hotter commodity.
"This lack of quality information has certainly been one of the driving factors behind the lack of investors'
confidence towards gold as an investment," he wrote.

--- Page 18 ---
Page 18 of 192 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
In an interview, Mr. Sprott called gold's recent drop in price "bizarre."
Two of the most-cited reasons for owning gold are to protect against inflation and to hedge against
plummeting stocks.
The first, inflation, has been rumored for years. So far, it hasn't arrived.
On Wednesday, the Labor Department said that prices rose only 1.2% in the year ended September.
But even if an investor is worried about future inflation, he might be better served somewhere else.
Indeed, even over long periods, gold has failed to keep up with prices.
Consider the 1980s and 1990s. In those two decades, gold's price fell from about $533 an ounce to $289,
according to SIX Financial, even as overall prices more than doubled. Even now, gold is still below its 1980s
inflation-adjusted peak of more than $2,300.
"It's one of those things that makes intuitive sense but turns out not to be true," says William Bernstein, an
investment manager at Efficient Frontier Advisors in Eastford, Conn.
Gold bought at other times has sometimes done a better job keeping up with prices. But if inflation
protection is really the goal, there are other options that address the problem much more directly, Mr.
Bernstein says.
For example, 10-year Treasury inflation-protected securities -- which are Treasurys whose principal rises
and falls with prices -- are back in positive territory after spending some time delivering negative returns after
inflation. TIPS currently yield 0.31% annually after inflation.
Small investors who don't want exposure to interest-rate risk can look to I-Savings Bonds, sold at
TreasuryDirect.gov, which right now are paying interest equal to the rate of inflation plus a small fixed rate.
Such bonds can be redeemed, with a small penalty, after only a year, but have a very low annual purchase
limit of $10,000 per investor.
Though they can go through stretches of declines, stocks actually have done a fine job beating inflation over
time. While inflation has run at about 3% a year between 1926 and 2012, large U.S. stocks have grown by
about 9.8% a year, according to Ibbotson Associates .
To be sure, stocks are more volatile than TIPS and I-bonds, but over the past five years, the S&P 500 has
been less volatile than SPDR Gold Shares , according to Morningstar.
Gold's other oft-cited role -- as a hedge against falling stock prices -- carries a little more weight, at least
when markets drop sharply. According to a Leuthold analysis, from 2006 to 2012, when the S&P 500 dropped
by 1% or more, gold tended to rise sharply.
That is one reason Charles de Vaulx, chief investment officer and portfolio manager at International Value
Advisers, keeps a small 3.25% allocation to the metal in the $9.5 billion IVA Worldwide Fund. If stocks
perform well and gold drops, the tiny position won't have a big impact on his fund. But if stocks plummet,
gold has the chance to double or triple, which could offset significant losses, he says.
"Within the context of a portfolio, something that can go up a lot when the rest of the portfolio goes down is a
wonderful thing," he says, adding that he thinks gold can also protect against currency debasement and
deflation. The IVA fund's A shares charge annual fees of 1.28%.
But experts warn that investors shouldn't go overboard. Mr. de Vaulx, for instance, views gold as a tiny part
of his cash position, to defend against stock-market declines.
Following that approach, an investor with an allocation of 60% to stocks and 30% to bonds would have only
the final 10% of cash out of which to carve out a gold allocation.
One percent, Leuthold's current allocation to gold, "is not a whole lot of portfolio diversification," Mr. Ramsey
says. "An allocation of zero probably isn't that much different."
Instead, if an investor fears a sharp market drop, there are other options besides the obvious bonds and
cash. He can set up a "stop loss" order to sell stocks when they fall by a set amount, say 10%, says Chris
Cook, president of Zero Commission Portfolios, which manages $450 million.
That limits losses without relying on gold to behave as expected, he says.

--- Page 19 ---
Page 19 of 192 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
"Gold has a personality disorder. You don't know what you're going to get," Mr. Cook says. "That creates a
problem for anyone trying to use it."

Friday's Markets
Blue Chips Advance Fourth Week in a Row
By Alexandra Scaggs

Copyright 2013 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Stocks gained as investors weighed a handful of better-than-expected reports on manufacturing activity
around the globe.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average added 69.80 points, or 0.5%, to 15615.55. The S&P 500-stock index
gained 5.10 points, or 0.3%, to 1761.64. The Nasdaq Composite Index edged up 2.34 points, or 0.1%, to
3922.04.
The Dow industrials and S&P 500 rebounded after a midweek retreat from record highs, to close out a fourth
consecutive week of gains. Friday's gains build on a 4.5% rally for the S&P 500 in October to leave the index
up 24% for the year so far.
A measure of U.S. manufacturing activity beat forecasts in October despite a partial government shutdown
and rose to its highest since April 2011. That followed a report from China that showed renewed strength in
its manufacturing sector, though growth remained unbalanced.
Investors and traders have been keeping a close eye on economic data in recent months, as they try to
determine when the Federal Reserve would start to pare back its easy-money policies, which many believe
have helped power this year's rally.
"Just being worked up [about the Fed] can make the market jump around," said John Manley, chief equity

economic growth will raise a little bit of concern."
After the year's strong gains, investors and strategists say the stock market could be more sensitive to
perceived changes in the outlook for the economy and Fed policy. "Investors are trying to figure out where
they are," said Michael O'Rourke , chief market strategist at brokerage JonesTrading Institutional Services .
"You have to be cautious in case the Fed does come out and" pare purchases.

stock-market bulls say that underlying economic strength and losses in the bond market could help support
stocks in the long term.

posted better-than-expected quarterly results.

projections.
Crude-oil futures slipped 1.8% to $94.61 a barrel. Gold futures dropped 0.8% to $1,313.10 a troy ounce. The
dollar gained against the euro and the yen.
Most European stock benchmarks declined but remained higher for the week. The Stoxx Europe 600 slipped
0.3% Friday but was up 0.4% for the week.
China's Shanghai Composite Index rose 0.4% as its purchasing managers index, an official reading on
manufacturing, rose to 51.4 in October, an 18-month high.

OFF DUTY --- Adventure & Travel: Queasy Rider --- A motorcycle newbie takes a rental Harley down winding, windy roads from Las Vegas to the...
OFF DUTY --- Adventure & Travel: Queasy Rider --- A motorcycle newbie takes a rental Harley down
winding, windy roads from Las Vegas to the Sierra Nevada
By John Jurgensen
1,540 words
2 November 2013
The Wall Street Journal
J
D9
English
Copyright 2013 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
The first time I rode a motorcycle on a street was when I steered a 650-pound Harley-Davidson into traffic in
Las Vegas. On that August morning, with sweat beading under my brand new goggles, my worst fear was
stalling the engine or teetering sideways in front of the bikers who had just filled out my rental paperwork. Any
concerns about tractor trailers, hairpin turns and other hazards took a back seat to the potential for injured
pride.
I slowly rolled back the throttle with my right hand, while carefully releasing my death grip on the clutch. With
a minor lurch and some gassy burbles from the hog's tailpipes, I eased out of the driveway and down the
street. I was officially a cliche on wheels: the man who greets middle age by getting his hands on a loud,
eye-catching vehicle.
For my 40th birthday a few months earlier, my wife had given me motorcycle lessons (and the best comeback
to everyone's first question: "What does your wife have to say about that?") Over three days at a Harley
dealership in New Jersey, there was classroom instruction, practice in a secluded parking lot and tough love
from the instructors about cornering. We maneuvered on lightweight, low-powered Buells -- the bike
equivalent of the docile ponies that kids ride at state fairs. After passing the final test, all I had to do was
report to the DMV to have my driver's license upgraded with an "M."
Despite all the trappings of a midlife crisis, my motivation was to join an old friend, Sam Ketay, on the kind of
multiday trips out West he'd been doing solo. The two of us used to go backpacking in the Adirondack
Mountains of upstate New York, but 50-pound packs and sore knees had started to make those hikes seem
like forced marches.
Now, the camping gear was bungeed to the backs of our motorcycles for a four-day trip. Sam, who's had a
motorcycle license since he was 18, planned a route that cut through the desert northwest of Las Vegas, then
climbed into the Sierra Nevada range in California and traversed Yosemite National Park. After looping back
east across the mountains, we would retrace our steps to Vegas. The plan promised empty roads, diverse
terrain and epic views. Although the desert heat was brutal in late summer, we'd be camping at higher, cooler
altitudes.
Eaglerider, the country's largest motorcycle-rental company, supplied bikes. "Stay off dirt roads -- we won't
come get you there if you get a flat tire," a staffer in Las Vegas warned us. "Don't drink and ride. Don't go to
Mexico."
Our Harleys had curvaceous figures, glossy black gas tanks and lots of chrome. Sam took a Fat Boy. Mine
was a slightly sleeker Low Rider. These are models made for laid-back cruising and long hauls, but they're
not loaded down with stereos or other plush accessories. I got used to the wind's battering ram on my chest --
I had declined the optional windshield -- and the occasional bug freckling my face.
We left Las Vegas on the relatively sedate Route 160. The clusters of identical houses and strip malls thinned
as we ascended into the Spring Mountains on the city's western edge. On the other side, the Mojave Valley
opened up and the road went straight off into the horizon. The valley floor bristled with dusty green creosote
bushes, and isolated hills looked like heaps of metal shavings. Riding in T-shirts in 100-degree heat, we got a
feel for what life might be like inside a convection oven.
A few hours later, after chili dogs at the Happy Burro in the quiet crossroads town of Beatty, and passing
many signs advertising beef jerky, fireworks and brothels, we climbed into the hills on Route 266. The
temperature dipped as we reached 7,500 feet and the ghost town of Lida; fossilized wooden shacks marked
where gold and silver was processed into the early 1900s.

--- Page 23 ---
Page 23 of 192 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Marathon car trips can be numbing, even with stunning scenery. Riding a motorcycle was a sensory blowout:
the sudden smell of sage as we slowed down for a water break, the needling pain of raindrops from a fleeting
shower.
At quiet Horton Creek Campground, on public land west of Bishop, Calif., we found a sandy hilltop site. We
settled in with the holy trinity of man-camping -- steaks cooked on the campfire, a flask of bourbon,
hand-rolled cigarettes -- as the moon lighted up the foothills. When we used to hike together, we normally
carried only one tent, to make our packs lighter. At least being 40 meant I got to sleep on my own. When I
closed my eyes,
I still saw asphalt blurring by.
The next day, we climbed into Yosemite via a ribbon of switchback turns. Riding along Tioga Road, which
traverses the park, we peered over steep drop-offs at green meadows hemmed in by towers of white granite.
At one point, I was so focused on the rhythm of shifting, braking and accelerating that I was shocked to look
up at a white mushroom cloud filling the blue sky -- a wildfire that had broken out north of the park. Our
intended path out of the western side of Yosemite was closed. We cut short our time among the rock towers
and tour buses and committed to exiting southwest to Mariposa, which would add 90 miles to the day.
After about eight hours on the bike and the relentless kinks of another mountain pass, I was seeing double as
we pulled into Jamestown at sunset. Far from our target campground, we settled for a cheap motel whose
only perk was its location -- a block from a saloon, the Willow. The bartender revived us with steaks and a
briefing worthy of "The X-Files," including tales of a mysterious electric shock in the desert that momentarily
paralyzed him, and the appearance of a local ghost in the flames of a cherries jubilee he once prepared.
The detour forced us to reroute the second leg of the trip, skipping Nevada's Route 50, aka the Loneliest
Road in America. But we still had a full montage of scenes ahead: Rolling through fog-thick smoke in the firs
and lodgepole pines of Stanislaus National Forest, where the so-called Rim Fire started from a hunter's
campfire; descending through the sagebrush surrounding Mono Lake, an alkaline-blue expanse at the edge
of the Great Basin.
During long stretches on the bike, my mind didn't follow typical routes. I barely thought about work or the
future. The thrumming motor, the wind's white noise and the landscape put me into a semi-meditative state.
The proper frame of mind, I thought, for entering the second half of my life.
We aimed for a dot on the map labeled Benton Hot Springs, hoping for rugged pools surrounded by
wilderness. Instead we found a 20-year-old B&B situated among the skeletal remains of a 19th-century silver
town. We set up our tents on a grassy site with a spring-fed tub made of cedar planks. The water eased our
saddle fatigue and offered a view of 13,000-foot Boundary Peak to the east.
On the last day, crosswinds walloped us as we cut through the flatlands around Pahrump, Nev. By that point
I'd gotten much friendlier with my bike. On empty straightaways, I hunkered down and wrenched the throttle. I
goosed the bike coming out of curves. I took longer looks at the scenery. This was no midlife crisis, just a
more rousing way to do a road trip.
---
THE LOWDOWN: MOTORCYCLING IN THE SIERRA NEVADA
Getting There: Most major airlines fly to Las Vegas, where Eaglerider can arrange shuttle pickups. The
company specializes in Harley-Davidsons (from $100 per day, eaglerider.com), but also rents BMWs, Hondas
and other makes. The company offers guided tours and self-guided tours with pre-loaded GPS units.
Staying There: Campgrounds are plentiful throughout the area. Some Bureau of Land Management sites,
such as Horton Creek Campground near Bishop, Calif., cost just $5 per night. Make reservations for
campgrounds inside Yosemite National Park. The Inn at Benton Hot Springs offers spring-fed tubs in every
room and in their grassy campsites (rooms from $109 a night, campsites from $40 a night,
historicbentonhotsprings.com).
Eating There: It seems every menu in the region has a page devoted to local lore. Tom's Place, a rustic resort
near Mammoth Mountain, offers big portions of biscuits and gravy (8181 Crowley Lake Dr., Crowley Lake,
Calif., tomsplaceresort.com). In Beatty, Nev., the Happy Burro is known for its savory chili and friendly
atmosphere (100 W. Main St.).
Recovering There: Back in Las Vegas, rinse off the road grime at a hotel spa. At the Monte Carlo, non-guests
can use the private showers, sauna and whirlpool for $22 (3770 Las Vegas Blvd. S., montecarlo.com)

The Wall Street Journal

Stocks fell in October's final session, as investors weighed upbeat corporate earnings against the potential for
the Federal Reserve to pare its stimulus efforts.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average declined 73.01 points, or 0.5%, to 15545.75, closing near session lows.
The S&P 500 dropped 6.77 points, or 0.4%, to 1756.54. The Nasdaq Composite Index slid 10.91 points, or

Thursday's decline marked a downbeat end to a big month for U.S. stocks. The S&P 500 rallied 4.5% in
October despite the 16-day government shutdown that gripped Washington and briefly cast doubt over U.S.
credit. Markets shrugged off the uncertainty in part because the shutdown pushed back expectations for
when the Fed might begin to wind down, or "taper," its monthly bond-purchase program.
Both the Dow and S&P 500 sit less than 1% below all-time highs, and investors are hesitant to call a top to
the market, especially with few hurdles on the horizon in the coming weeks. The S&P is up 23% this year.
"Cheap money has been fueling this puppy, and the cheap money is going to stay around for a while," said
Martin Leclerc, chief investment officer at Barrack Yard Advisors in Bryn Mawr, Pa. "It seems like the path of
least resistance is still higher."
Possible changes to Fed policy remain a focus. On Wednesday, the central bank's assessment of the U.S.
economy was upbeat, leaving open the possibility that the Fed could still begin to reduce its bond-buying
efforts in December.
The S&P 500 slipped 0.5% on Wednesday to break a four-day winning streak.
Exxon Mobil rose 81 cents, or 0.9%, to $89.62 after the company's third-quarter net income and revenue
topped Wall Street's estimates.

Visa dropped 7.15, or 3.5%, to 196.67 after the debit- and credit-card transaction processor said that sales

In Europe, stocks recovered from early losses, with the Stoxx Europe 600 index gaining 0.5%.
The yield on the 10-year Treasury note rose to 2.542%, from 2.526% late Wednesday.

Stocks Retreat After Fed
Wednesday's Markets
Stocks Retreat After Fed
By Alexandra Scaggs and Min Zeng
514 words
31 October 2013
The Wall Street Journal
J
C4
English
Copyright 2013 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Stocks backed away from record highs after the Federal Reserve left its assessment of the economy
relatively unchanged, surprising investors and leading to speculation the central bank could begin scaling
back stimulus efforts at year-end.
Many market watchers were expecting the Fed to downgrade its economic outlook due to budget wrangling in
Washington earlier this month that caused a government shutdown.
The fact that it didn't raises the likelihood that the central bank could pare, or "taper," stimulus measures as
early as its next policy-setting meeting, scheduled for December. At the meeting that concluded Wednesday,
the Fed made no change to its $85 billion-a-month bond-buying program.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average declined 61.59 points, or 0.4%, to 15618.76, with 25 of 30 components
lower. On Tuesday, the Dow rose 111 points to beat out the previous record high from September. The S&P
500 index fell 8.64 points, or 0.5%, to 1763.31. The Nasdaq Composite Index was down 21.72 points, or
0.5%, to 3930.62.
"The Fed didn't seem to see the government shutdown and fiscal retrenchment as a big concern for the
economy," said Jason Evans, co-founder of hedge fund NineAlpha Capital LP in New York. "If the Fed is right
about the underlying strength of the U.S. economy, the taper timing could be earlier than many people
expected. A December taper is not completely out of the question. But the bar is high."
Bonds and gold also fell, while the dollar strengthened against major currencies. The moves indicated that
investors across a broad swathe of markets readjusted their expectations for when the Fed would taper.
Yields on 10-year Treasurys rose as high as 2.549%, compared with 2.485% ahead of the Fed statement.
When yields rise, bond prices fall.
"Many people thought that the Fed would mention that the government shutdown would be a drag on
growth," said Dan Mulholland, head of U.S. Treasury trading at BNY Mellon Capital Markets .
While the Fed didn't mention any slowdown from the partial government shutdown earlier this month, data
hasn't indicated that growth is picking up, particularly in the labor market, which Fed officials have said will
play a key factor in policy decisions. On Wednesday, private-sector payrolls reported by Automatic Data
Processing and Moody's Analytics were lower than expected, and the consumer-price index indicated that
inflation wasn't picking up.
"The labor data right now is extraordinarily weak," said Phil Orlando , chief equity market strategist at
Federated Investors, which manages $367 billion. "We need to see some really strong reports to meet the
threshold" for the Fed to start to cut back on its bond-buying programs, he said.
The declines continued in Asia early Thursday. Japan's Nikkei was down 0.3%, Australia's S&P/ASX 200 was
down 0.1% and South Korea's Kospi was down 0.7%. The Shanghai composite opened down 0.2%.

'Idiot-Proof': Billionaire's Empire Soared, Then Melted Into Bankruptcy
'Idiot-Proof': Billionaire's Empire Soared, Then Melted Into Bankruptcy
By John Lyons, Luciana Magalhaes and Loretta Chao
2,716 words
31 October 2013
The Wall Street Journal
J
A1
English
Copyright 2013 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
RIO DE JANEIRO -- In 2006, Eike Batista strode into Rio's opulent Copacabana Palace hotel for cocktails
with a Canadian pension fund looking to play Brazil's rise. The Brazilian entrepreneur captivated his hosts
with a brazen pitch: He aimed to be the world's richest man, and investors along for the ride would prosper,
too.
"The way he says it, it doesn't sound crazy," says Brian Gibson , then a senior vice president with the Ontario
Teacher's Pension Plan, which organized the reception.
"As an investor, you are looking for someone who is hungry," Mr. Gibson says. "I didn't know if he was going
to be the richest or not, and I didn't care."
Mr. Batista didn't make it. Instead, he presided over one of the most breathtaking rise-and-fall stories in the
history of business. Starting in 2006, Mr. Batista publicly listed five startups in five years, creating a Brazilian
commodities empire from scratch. He called the companies "idiot-proof" and gave them names ending in "X"
to mean multiplication of wealth. The 56-year-old's backers ranged from General Electric 's chief executive,
Jeff Immelt , to portfolio giant BlackRock Inc . By last year Mr. Batista ascended to the Top 10 of the Forbes
list, with wealth of more than $30 billion.
Then, his empire fell. The OGX oil firm he took public in 2008 for $4.1 billion -- then Brazil's largest initial
public offering of stock -- declared on Wednesday Latin America's biggest ever bankruptcy after failing to
produce nearly any of the 10.8 billion barrels Mr. Batista claimed it could find. The decline of OGX sent
shares tumbling across the rest of his empire, wiping out most of his wealth.
On Wednesday, OGX shares fell 26%. The shares are down 96% over the past 12 months.
Losers in Mr. Batista's misadventures include bond giant Pacific Investment Management Co . and portfolio
manager BlackRock, among the biggest buyers of $3.6 billion of OGX bonds now worth pennies on the dollar.
Among the few winners are funds such as Ontario Teachers, which says it got out early, as well as OGX
executives who left the company while its shares were flying high.
Mr. Batista says he still has at least $1 billion, although that is impossible to verify given the complexity of his
dealings. He's off the Forbes list.
During a September interview in his Rio office, Mr. Batista said he had been misled by his executives. "I gave
out the information people gave me," he said, stating that he has lost more money than anyone else.
Sitting amid the detritus of a half-decade of deal-making -- maps of his operations, an electric guitar in the
shape of Brazil -- he predicted a comeback. "My projects are alive and will become benchmarks." Out the
window, one of his oil platforms sat in the distance in Rio's bay.
Mr. Batista's investors, including Abu Dhabi's sovereign-wealth fund and the Brazilian government, are trying
to recoup some of the $20 billion in debt and equity they gave him. Hanging over the effort is a larger
question: How did such blue-chip names put so much so fast into one man's ideas, only to have them prove
such grandiose failures?
Part of the answer lies in the wave of optimism that swept over Brazil as Mr. Batista hit the scene. Global
investors were flocking to resource-rich Brazil as commodities surged and the U.S. and European economies
slowed. A record number of Brazilian companies sold shares in 2007, mostly to foreign buyers; a year in
which Brazil's stock market was among the world's biggest gainers. If that weren't enough, just as crude oil
prices climbed toward records, Brazil made the hemisphere's biggest oil find in decades.

--- Page 28 ---
Page 28 of 192 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Part of the answer also lies in Mr. Batista's ability to channel the optimism toward his business ideas. Several
of his associates described him as keenly aware of the incentives of the bankers, journalists, investors and
Brazilian officials caught up in the boom. He told executives to inspire "greed," several of them said.
Mr. Batista denies telling his executives to inspire greed.
Mr. Batista had big local backing as well. President Dilma Rousseff presided over a ceremony to mark the
beginning of OGX oil production, praising Mr. Batista and calling OGX a potential "partner" with state-owned
Petroleos Brasileiros SA. The state development bank BNDES lent him some $3 billion. Partly as a result,
some investors viewed him as too big to fail.
BNDES declined to comment.
For many, the charismatic man with fuzzy hair implants and pink ties was the face of Brazil's boom. Born in
Brazil to a Brazilian father and German mother, Mr. Batista speaks fluent English tinged with a German
accent. His Rio offices became a required stop for investors on scouting trips to Brazil.
He has been featured on "60 Minutes" and other shows doing specials on Brazil's rise. TV hosts often
introduced him as the man seeking to pass Bill Gates or Mexico's Carlos Slim as the richest in the world,
depending on who was then first.
"I don't know whether I'll pass him on the left, or pass him on the right," Mr. Batista sometimes joked back.
Mr. Batista's personality fit the go-go verve of Brazil's rise, investors said. He had married a top Brazilian
sex-symbol and parked a $1 million Mercedes-Benz SLR McLaren in his living room. He talked about his
astrological map and bought oil rights for amounts ending in 63, his lucky number.
"It was a bubble scam," says Aurelio Valporto, a semiretired economist and former securities trader who says
he lost 40% of his savings in Mr. Batista's OGX oil company. "I can deal with losing the money, because I've
lost money before. What eats at me is this feeling of being robbed."
Brazil's securities regulator is now investigating possible insider trading by Mr. Batista, who denies any
wrongdoing and says he never misled investors.
Other investors say Mr. Batista is a visionary who believed in his companies and had most of his wealth tied
up in them as they went down. They say he lacked the managerial skill to guide an empire that expanded
rapidly in a business climate where just about any idea he had got funded.
"He was a seller of dreams, and he is going down with his dreams," says Arminio Fraga, a former Brazilian
central-bank president whose hedge fund profited by investing around $40 million in OGX during a
private-equity round and then selling after its IPO.
With crucial early backing from Ontario Teachers, Mr. Batista raised money for an interlinking conglomerate
of resource and infrastructure startups. His MMX mining firm said it would ship iron ore to China through a
superport, bigger than Manhattan, being constructed by LLX, his logistics company. A power company, MPX,
would provide the juice. The OSX shipbuilding firm would locate a shipyard at the LLX port and build oil
platforms for OGX, the oil firm.
Together, the companies' shares provided investors easy ways to play the various sectors of Brazil's boom.
The only problem is, some seven years after his first public listing, the empire isn't profitable.
He started other businesses, too. He bought Rio's historic Hotel Gloria and promised to revamp it, a still
unfinished project. He started a party-boat business called "Pink Fleet," which filed for bankruptcy. He
invested in mixed martial arts fighting.
Mr. Batista owns a Chinese restaurant in Rio, called Mr. Lam, which was packed on a recent Tuesday night.
He said he was partnering with IBM to start the Southern Hemisphere's largest semiconductor manufacturer.
IBM declined to comment.
In the September interview, seated at the conference table in a dark suit and a gray designer T-shirt, Mr.
Batista denied he misled investors about his prospects. "The main mistake was that the resources that were
supposed to be there were not productive," he said.
He said OGX's failure to find oil caused a domino effect of selling that was spurred on by rumors from former
associates who wanted to bring him down. "No one can withstand a bank run," Mr. Batista said. "And with all
those people talking, how many of them wanted to take control of the assets for themselves?"
He said many of his projects have promising futures and were unfairly tarnished by OGX's collapse. For
example, EIG Global Energy Services paid $560 million for a stake in his logistics firm LLX. Mr. Batista sold a

--- Page 29 ---
Page 29 of 192 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
stake in power company MPX to the German E.ON SE , and it was renamed Eneva. And his MMX mining
company sold a port in Southern Brazil to Trafigura Beheer BV and the Abu Dhabi sovereign-wealth fund
Mubdala in a $400 million deal.
One of the attractions to investing with Mr. Batista was the possibility that he would bail out his companies
with his own wealth. In Oct. 2012, he granted a "put" option to OGX that required him to personally buy $1
billion worth of shares at a 36% premium to where the stock was trading. He had made a similar promise to
purchase $1 billion of OSX shares.
He paid part of the OSX "put," and none of the promise to OGX. In the interview, he said the drastic
deterioration of OGX changed the terms of his promise.
Now that OGX has filed for bankruptcy protection, Mr. Batista has 60 days to present a reorganization plan.
Holders of its $3.6 billion bonds are likely to wind up with control of the company. The company has yet to
start producing oil and its previous attempts to do so have failed.
Mr. Batista, meantime, has sought to persuade creditors of his OSX shipbuilding company, including big
Brazilian government-run banks, to roll over its debts until buyers can be found for its platforms, which Mr.
Batista says are worth $1 billion.
In April, Anglo-American Mining PLC's chief executive, Cynthia Carroll , stepped down in part because the
board deemed her $4.6 billion purchase of a Batista mining prospect in 2007 a mistake that hurt the
company. Mr. Batista called the sale a "huge success." Once Anglo American took possession, the company
found it needed another $8.8 billion to become operational, far more than it thought.
Mrs. Carroll has said that Brazil's policies and regulations have been constantly evolving and that she isn't
sure she could have anticipated some of the problems.
Some investment banks, including Credit Suisse , that brought some of Mr. Batista's biggest deals to market
earned millions of dollars in fees. Ontario Teachers, Mr. Batista's early backer, says it booked a "10 digit"
profit on Mr. Batista.
There were warning signs. By the time Mr. Batista showed up at the Copacabana Palace to meet with Ontario
Teachers, he had already racked up a string of busts including a beer company, a jeep company called JPX
and a FedEx knockoff. There was also a door-to-door cosmetics company he had branded around the fame
of his first wife, Luma de Oliveira, a Playboy cover model who once famously showed up at Carnival wearing
a diamond-studded choker spelling out Mr. Batista's first name, "Eike."
One of Mr. Batista's most prominent busts was a gold company, TVX, which ranked among the world's
biggest gold miners in the 1980s and 1990s. But it crashed, and its working mines sold off, after Mr. Batista
made bad bets to expand into Greece and Russia.
Businessmen who have been pitched by Mr. Batista say he often starts the same way: With his early years as
a gold trader in the Brazilian Amazon. The point of the story is that Mr. Batista is a self-made man whose
vision is matched by risk-taking and perseverance.
By his own account, Mr. Batista got gold fever early. The second of seven children born to a senior Brazilian
official, he became fascinated with gold leafing through illustrations of Spanish Conquistadores who
conquered the Aztec and Incan empires in early 16th centuries.
"I could see myself with gold in my hands, falling through the fingers, a latter-day explorer of the 20th
century," Mr. Batista wrote in his 2011 autobiography.
He got his chance when a late-1970s gold rush took hold in the Amazon. He dropped out of an engineering
program at Aachen, Germany. Riding an old DC-3, Mr. Batista brought Amazon gold to Rio for sale. He says
he made $6 million in a year and half. He was still in his early 20s.
While the young Mr. Batista was in the Amazon, his father, Eliezer Batista, was helping build Vale SA into
one of the world's biggest iron-ore firms. The elder Batista ran Vale for a time, and the company created a
vast network of mines, trains and ports. Today, the elder Mr. Batista is considered one of the most prominent
men of his generation.
Observers seeking clues to Mr. Batista's ambition often look to his relationship with his father. Associates say
it is a rich mix of affection and competition. When the young Mr. Batista told his father he planned to bring
mechanized gold mining to the remote, malarial reaches of the Amazon, his father issued him an "idiot's
diploma," the younger Mr. Batista said, a remark he "didn't find funny."

--- Page 30 ---
Page 30 of 192 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Where the elder Mr. Batista was a revered government official, the younger Mr. Batista forged himself as a
brash free-market entrepreneur.
"When God gave out the gifts to my father, he gave him everything because he is a renaissance man. . .he
left one for me, the talent to make money," Mr. Batista told Brazilian television in 2010.
Mr. Batista's talent for moneymaking came into focus almost immediately after he walked out of the
Copacabana meeting with Ontario Teachers. The Canadian pension fund helped back an expansion of Mr.
Batista's MMX mining operations, part of which was sold to Anglo American . Then the fund helped Mr.
Batista launch his logistics and port operation, LLX.
But by far his biggest deal was launching OGX, the oil company now filing for bankruptcy protection. It raised
more than $9 billion in debt and equity since 2007, and became the flagship of his holding company, which he
called EBX.
The idea for the company was hatched aboard a British Airways flight in 2006. During a flight to Europe, Mr.
Batista talked oil with Rodolfo Landim, a former executive at Brazil's oil giant Petroleos Brasileiros SA who he
had hired to run his mining operation. Mr. Landim declined to comment.
The upshot was that Brazil had quietly discovered some of the richest new oil finds in the world, deep under
miles of ocean, rock and salt. Some of these fields were coming up for auction.
Mr. Batista resolved to raise money to buy some of these deep-water fields. The strategy was to acquire the
fields and then bring in the world's major oil companies to run them. In a matter of a few months, Mr. Batista
launched OGX with $500 million from Ontario Teachers and $500 million of his own money.
On paper, OGX wasn't an easy sell to investors. The deep-water fields were on a new frontier in the oil
industry, and OGX had no operations. Mr. Batista hired senior executives from Petrobras who had been part
of the company's deep-water discovery. One man, Paulo Mendonca , was a former head of exploration. Mr.
Batista introduced him to investors as "Dr. Oil." The assumption was that Mr. Batista's "dream team" had an
inside bead on what fields to bid for.
"The pitch was always about the people," said Mr. Fraga, the former Brazilian central banker who participated
in OGX's private-equity round of fundraising. "But the question is, if you have all of these good people, why
didn't they find any oil?"

Gold Fades From Investment Picture --- Central Banks Cut Purchases, Adding to Downward Pressure on the Metal Due to Gains in Stocks, Bonds
Commodities Report
Gold Fades From Investment Picture --- Central Banks Cut Purchases, Adding to Downward Pressure
on the Metal Due to Gains in Stocks, Bonds
By Matt Day and Francesca Freeman
972 words
29 October 2013
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2013 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
The investor gold rush that propelled the precious metal to a dozen years of annual price gains is on the
verge of ending with a whimper.
Russia's central bank in September sold gold for the first time in a year, according to the latest data from the
International Monetary Fund . Since the start of 2010, Russia has accounted for 30% of all gold purchases
made by central banks that report to the IMF.
Like other emerging-market nations, Russia bought gold to diversify its foreign-exchange reserves. The
retrenchment of Russia and others is the latest factor to weigh on gold prices, which are down 19% year to
date. The last time gold prices posted an annual loss was 2000.
Gold's rise was powered initially by the introduction of financial products in the mid-2000s that made the
metal more accessible to investors of all stripes. After the financial crisis, gains accelerated as investors
snapped up gold amid fears that central banks' easy-money policies would stoke inflation and diminish the
value of paper currencies.
Five years later, inflation remains subdued, dimming gold's allure.
Gold, which yields nothing and can be costly to hold, also is looking less attractive compared with more
mainstream investments. The widespread belief among many investors and analysts that the Federal
Reserve will scale back its bond-buying program soon has boosted bond yields, and steady U.S. economic
growth has brought major stock indexes back to record territory.
"Gold really doesn't have much to offer," said Joseph Murphy, a senior analyst who helps manage about $2
billion at Hermes Commodities, a unit of Hermes Fund Managers Ltd . in London. Hermes has trimmed its
gold holdings this year. "People are seeing better opportunities, whether that be in bonds or equities."
Big-name hedge-fund managers have been caught in this year's downdraft. The gold fund managed by John
Paulson 's Paulson & Co . has lost money. In June, veteran stock picker Jeffery Vinik closed his hedge fund
and returned several billion dollars to investors after poor performance tied to an ill-timed bet on gold-mining
stocks, which tend to track gold prices.
Central banks sold gold regularly until 2009. As a group, they became net buyers in 2010. The shift was
driven by emerging-market central banks, which were grappling with rapidly rising foreign-exchange reserves
that were a result of large trade surpluses.
But this year the tide has turned. Because of slowing economic growth and investors' increasing preference
for assets in the developed world, these emerging-market central banks have been using cash reserves to
stem economic turmoil and support their currencies. That leaves fewer dollars available to buy gold.
Central banks are on track to cut back their gold-buying by 34% in 2013, according to forecasts by metals
consulting firm Thomson Reuters GFMS. The retrenchment follows two consecutive years of rising
purchases, according to the IMF.
Russia sold 12,000 ounces of gold in September, the first such sale in a year, according to IMF data.
This year's decline in gold prices has prompted cautious central banks to stick to the sidelines until the
market becomes more stable, analysts and bankers say.

--- Page 32 ---
Page 32 of 192 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
"It really does blunt the support that gold has," said Jeff Wright , an analyst with H.C. Wainwright & Co.
"Purchasing by central banks was the fail safe, the line in the sand [supporting prices in] a volatile market. It's
a very important shift."
This year through August, central banks increased their gold stockpiles by 6.2 million troy ounces, compared
with 9.6 million ounces in the same period in 2012, according to the IMF.
The IMF collects details on gold holdings through voluntary reports from central banks. While the data is
considered accurate for most countries, analysts say China's reported reserves of 33.9 million ounces of gold
-- the world's fifth-highest -- likely underestimates the real total. Overall, the IMF estimates central banks own
just over one billion ounces of gold, or about 29,000 metric tons.
To be sure, most central banks are holding on to the gold they have bought, and many analysts expect them
to continue building their reserves of the metal, if at a slower pace. Prices have begun to creep up off their
summer lows, as investors push back the date when they expect the Fed to start tightening. Gold futures
were little changed on Monday, closing at $1,352 an ounce on the Comex division of the New York Mercantile
Exchange .
Speaking at a gold industry event in September, central-bank officials from France, Germany and Argentina
said that gold's volatility hadn't prompted any of their banks to plan to sell the metal.
Central-bank officials say they buy gold as part of an investment strategy whose time is measured in
decades. They view gold holdings in a similar light as their hoard of dollars or yen: as a backstop to help
keep economic conditions stable, or a tool to intervene in currency markets. Whether they have made or lost
money on their investment is secondary, many say.
"We don't follow trends," said Juan Ignacio Basco, deputy general manager of the central bank of Argentina,
speaking on the panel. "We need to be very cautious and move slowly."
Still, investors say it will be more difficult for gold prices to rally with central-bank appetites on the wane.
"Investors -- regardless of whether they are retail investors or central banks -- can behave like sheep," said
Christoph Eibl, chief executive of commodities-focused Tiberius Asset Management, which oversees $1.55
billion.
---
Tatyana Shumsky contributed to this article.

Investors Regain A Taste For China
Abreast of the Market
Investors Regain A Taste For China
By Shen Hong and Daniel Inman
845 words
28 October 2013
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2013 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
SHANGHAI -- Investors are turning their focus back to China, after months in which global markets were
driven by relentless speculation about the U.S. Federal Reserve's next move.
Shares are climbing across Asia, and prices for industrial commodities such as copper and iron ore have
bounced back amid signs that China's economy is revving up. The yuan regularly sets record highs against
the dollar as cash floods into the country.
It hasn't been an entirely smooth ride. Emerging-market stocks and bonds shuddered and gold prices jumped
last week as borrowing costs soared in China's money market, raising fears of a cash crunch for banks.
Some investors worry that official efforts to avoid a debt bubble and keep a lid on inflation could stifle growth
-- fears that sent Shanghai's stock market and global commodity prices tumbling earlier this year.
The willingness to take another chance on Chinese stocks and on other markets tightly linked to the country's
economy underscores the paucity of attractive options available. Global growth continues to be soft, while
hefty central-bank support for Western economies has resulted in near record prices in many stock and
bond markets. Though China's economic rise drove global markets for much of the past decade, the recent
rough patch presents a fresh opportunity to get in, some investors say.
"China comes along with a story -- a real bombed-out market, with low valuations -- and a lot of people
jumped on that," said Gary Dugan , chief investment officer, Asia and Middle East at private bank Coutts &
Co. Ltd., who raised his allocations to Chinese stocks in September.
The benchmark Shanghai Composite index has rallied 15% off of this year's low in late June to end Friday at
2132.96, although it is still down 6% on the year. In Indonesia, which counts China as a major customer for its
commodities, the Jakarta Stock Exchange Composite Index is up 12.7% this month. Prices of copper, for
which China makes up 40% of global demand, are up nearly 9% since hitting a three-year low in June, ending
last week at $7,180 a metric ton on the London Metal Exchange.
Those markets have gotten a boost from encouraging Chinese economic data recently. A private-sector
gauge of China's manufacturing activity jumped to a seven-month high this month, while the economy
expanded 7.8% in the third quarter from a year earlier, faster than growth in the first two quarters of 2013.
Adding fuel to the fire are the recent launch of a free-trade zone in Shanghai and expectations for economic
overhauls at a Communist Party meeting next month. The changes are expected to include greater access to
the economy for private and foreign investors, further economic deregulation and moves to allow increased
mobility of the population.
The indicators have eased concerns that China's growth rate will slow dramatically this year. China's booming
economy in recent years fueled demand for commodities ranging from oil to soybeans and lifted the
prospects of emerging markets. As the world's second-biggest economy has cooled, it has left global markets
increasingly dependent on the easy-money policies of rich countries' central banks, which may begin to
remove that support as early as next year.
"You began to see data that told you things weren't as bad as everyone thought," said Catherine Raw,
portfolio manager at BlackRock Inc .'s Commodities Strategies Fund, which oversees $420 million. "The fears
that China was going to have a hard landing . . . have disappeared."
Ms. Raw purchased copper-mining stocks early in the year, in a bet that a downturn in Chinese demand for
the metal would be temporary. She called the position "painful" until the market rebounded over the summer.
Still, jitters abound, mostly centered on China's lending market. The Shanghai Composite fell 2.8% last week,
and global commodities prices dropped as well after Chinese banks' funding costs shot up.

--- Page 34 ---
Page 34 of 192 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
The average rate on seven-day repurchase agreements, or repos, a benchmark that tracks the cost of
lending between banks, hit 5.05% on Friday, up from 3.49% a week earlier. The selloff has spread to
government bonds, too, with the 10-year government-bond yield climbing to 4.2% Friday, the highest level in
nearly six years.
Traders said the rapid rise in borrowing costs echoed June's crippling cash crunch, which left banks
scrambling for funds and spooked global markets.
It also brought to the forefront lingering worries about an explosion of debt, especially of lending to local
governments.
Early Asian Trading
Japan's Nikkei was up 1.1%, while Australia's S&P/ASX 200 was up 1.3% and South Korea's Kospi was flat
early Monday amid optimism that U.S. policy makers will continue monetary stimulus after weak U.S. data
last week. The Shanghai composite opened flat.

Precious Metals: Indian Buyers Pay a Premium for Gold
Precious Metals: Indian Buyers Pay a Premium for Gold
By Biman Mukherji and Debiprasad Nayak
373 words
26 October 2013
The Wall Street Journal
J
B5
English
Copyright 2013 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
NEW DELHI -- Gold buyers in India are paying big margins over the international price, with choked-up
supplies and festival-season demand to blame.
Wholesale buyers such as jewelers are paying up to $150 a troy ounce over the international price, excluding
taxes, local traders said, compared with about $3 a year ago and $50 a couple of weeks ago.
India, which imports almost all of the gold it consumes, levies a 10% import tax, increasing costs further for
local buyers. Add to that the sales tax and value-added tax, and gold was selling at 32,000 rupees ($520) per
10 grams, or $1,617 an ounce, Friday, up about 6% in October. Gold futures rose 0.2% to $1,352.40 an
ounce Friday on the Comex division of the New York Mercantile Exchange .
Demand peaks around Diwali -- the festival of lights, celebrated this year on Nov. 4 -- because of a Hindu
belief that buying gold during the period brings good luck. Demand usually remains strong through December
as the wedding season follows the festival period.
Vasu Acharya, director of Parker Bullions, said gold wasn't available even at higher premiums. Ketan Shroff ,
director of Penta Gold Pvt. Ltd., a Mumbai-based bullion dealer, agreed. "Our business has completely dried
up this Diwali," he said. "The reason is there is no supply."
Jewelers said the supply squeeze is affecting smaller buyers more because they usually replenish their
stocks on short notice depending on demand.
"We don't know how we will celebrate Diwali this year," said Ashish Pethe, a partner of Waman Hari Pethe
Jewellers.
The shortage of gold follows measures aimed at curbing its consumption in order to narrow India's trade and
current-account deficits. The tax on gold imports rose to 10% from 6%, and the central bank required
importers starting in July to ensure 20% of gold they import is exported after adding value, such as by being
made into jewelry.
The value of gold imports in September was down 82% to $800 million, helping narrow India's trade deficit to
a 30-month low, but also leading to the current shortage.
