Thursday's Markets
Dow's Skid Brings It Under 15000 --- Blue Chips Post 9th Decline in 11 Sessions as Investors Grapple
With Prospect of Extended Shutdown
By Dan Strumpf and Min Zeng
928 words
4 October 2013
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2013 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Stocks fell broadly as investors weighed the consequences of an extended government shutdown.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average fell 136.66 points, or 0.9%, to 14996.48, its ninth decline in 11 sessions.
All but four Dow components were lower.
Traders said the mood across Wall Street was cautious as the shutdown of the federal government entered
its third day and the deadline to raise the nation's borrowing limit drew closer.
For the most part, investors expect Congress and the White House to come to terms on a deal that will
reopen the government and avoid the federal government running into the debt ceiling, which could lead to
the Treasury Department being unable to meet its debt payments.
In the meantime, however, investors increasingly are weighing short-term negative effects of the government
shutdown on the economy.
The S&P 500 index gave up 15.21 points, or 0.9%, to 1678.66, and the Nasdaq Composite Index shed 40.68
points, or 1.1%, to 3774.34.
While the selloff of recent days picked up steam Thursday, the S&P 500 is still up 17.7% so far in 2013 and
just 2.7% below its record high set Sept. 18.
In the bond market, trading in short-term U.S. debt known as Treasury bills showed a more direct impact of
worries about possible disruptions from hitting the debt ceiling. Yields on super-short term Treasury bills--
those maturing in October-- increased to their highest levels since a budget standoff in the summer of 2011.
Despite the jitters at the very short end of the bond market, longer-dated Treasurys benefited from investors
buying government debt as a haven.
The yield on the 10-year Treasury note inched lower to 2.606% from 2.624% late Wednesday.
The dollar fell amid expectations that the economic impact of the government shutdown will keep the Federal
Reserve on its current easy-money course. The WSJ Dollar Index, which measures the dollar against a
basket of currencies, has dropped five days in a row.
The Dow was down as much as 186 points before recovering some of the lost ground following a report that
House Speaker John Boehner told Republican colleagues that he wouldn't allow the U.S. to default on its
debt.
"I'm not sitting here expecting a default," said Chris Bertelsen, chief investment officer of Global Financial
Private Capital, a $2.3 billion money manager in Sarasota, Fla.
"It's hard to guess what goes on as far as the political situation is concerned, but from an investment
viewpoint there are some opportunities this has created," Mr. Bertelsen said.
Despite the extended rally in stocks, Mr. Bertelsen still thinks certain stocks are cheap. For example, he
began buying shares of Hertz Global Holdings Inc . this week.
Still, there were signs the impact of the shutdown was spilling over into the broader economy.
Dow component Boeing Co . slid $2.60, or 2.2%, to $115.24 after it warned that deliveries of some of its jets
could be delayed as a result of the partial government shutdown.

--- Page 64 ---
Page 64 of 192 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Fellow blue chip United Technologies Corp. dropped 1.29, or 1.2%, to 103.69 after the defense contractor
said it would be forced to furlough 2,000 workers at a facility that makes Black Hawk helicopters for the
Defense Department.
Jim Anderson, investment specialist at J.P. Morgan Private Bank, which oversees about $910 billion, said a
shutdown of two or three weeks would be "a nick to GDP."
"The debt ceiling is another matter," he said, calling it "an uncertainty with greater danger." He said he exited
some stock positions ahead of the shutdown, citing the political uncertainty and stocks' steep gains recently.
In the bond market, corporate treasurers, money-market-fund managers, some foreign central banks and
financial firms have been unloading very short-term Treasury bills due on October 17, 24 and 31, traders and
investors said.
Those maturities cluster around Oct. 17, the deadline for U.S. lawmakers to boost the nation's $16.7 trillion
debt ceiling and avoid defaulting on Treasury debt payments.
The wave of Treasury-bill selling has created an anomaly in which yields on some bills maturing in a month
pay interest that is double or even triple that on bills maturing in three to six months.
The yield on the Treasury bill due Oct. 31 rose as high as 0.167% Thursday, according to Tradeweb. That
marked the highest yield since late July 2011, the last time there was a threat of the federal government
running into the debt ceiling. Bond yields rise when their prices drop.
"The default risk remains very small but investors are hedging the risk of an accidental missing of interest
payments" by the Treasury, said Stanley Sun, interest-rates strategist at Nomura Securities International in
New York.
October gold futures fell 0.2% to $1,317.40 a troy ounce, pulling back after the previous session's sharp
gains.
November crude-oil futures eased 0.8% to settle at $103.31 a barrel.
The dollar slipped against both the yen and the euro.
In Asia early Friday, Japan's Nikkei was down 1%, Hong Kong's Hang Seng index was down 0.9%, South
Korea's Kospi was down 0.6% and Australia's S&P/ASX 200 was down 0.4%.

There's Gold On That Thar Capitol Hill
Heard on the Street
There's Gold On That Thar Capitol Hill
By Liam Denning
331 words
2 October 2013
The Wall Street Journal
J
C16
English
Copyright 2013 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
[Financial Analysis and Commentary]
Gold bugs love a crisis, but they need it to last.
On day one of Washington's shutdown, gold fell 3%, slipping again below $1,300 an ounce. That might seem
odd, given the metal is often viewed as insurance against bouts of governmental madness.
But it actually fits broadly with history. Renaissance Macro Research looked at how markets moved during 17
previous shutdowns between 1976 and 1996. There is a low, but noticeable, positive correlation between how
long a shutdown lasts and how much gold goes up. For the six shutdowns lasting three days or less, for
example, five saw gold prices drop by an average of 1.1%. By contrast, of the five lasting 10 days or more,
three saw gold rally by an average of 3.5%.
As so often these days, though, the Federal Reserve's role is critical.
Gold's biggest gain, of 5.5%, came during the 18-day shutdown in the fall of 1978. But back then, inflation
was north of 8.5% and rising. Meanwhile, real interest rates -- Treasury yields minus inflation -- were falling
rapidly toward zero and beyond. This was perfect for gold, as it is a haven and inflation hedge. And the
opportunity cost of holding such a nonyielding asset dwindles as real rates turn negative.
Today, inflation is moribund and, given the damage a shutdown may do to the economy, the bigger risk may
be deflation. Real rates, which turned sharply negative after the financial crisis and fueled gold's earlier rally,
have started rising and could soon turn positive, especially if the Fed starts tapering bond purchases soon.
An extended shutdown or a debt-ceiling debacle could well stay the Fed's hand and stoke fear in general.
Most Americans might gnash their teeth at that prospect. But for gold's biggest fans, things may need to get
much worse before they get better.

Shutting Down Emerging Markets
Heard on the Street
Shutting Down Emerging Markets
By Liam Denning
491 words
1 October 2013
The Wall Street Journal
J
C12
English
Copyright 2013 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
[Financial Analysis and Commentary]
American exceptionalism remains alive and well in the bond market at least: When it looks like the U.S.
government might shut down, its cost of borrowing actually falls.
Officials in emerging markets can look on only in envy. They have more to fear from furloughs in Washington
than Americans do.
Stock markets reacted Monday as the realization set in that the U.S. federal government might really close
temporarily. Yet the yield on 10-year Treasury notes actually fell slightly. Moreover, looking at those stock
markets, the S&P 500 fell by less than 1%, but stocks in Brazil, Hong Kong and India all dropped by 1.5% or
more.
In part, this reflects the fact that a temporary government shutdown of one or two weeks would be negative
for the U.S. economy but not an immediate disaster. Tellingly, gold, the traditional insurance against
government curveballs, didn't rally Monday.
Indeed, a shutdown could help break Washington's partisan fever and lessen the threat of an impasse over
raising the federal debt ceiling. This is potentially more serious, as it raises the possibility, however remote, of
a U.S. default.
Besides these considerations, though, the relative reactions of markets speak to asymmetry: Investors simply
treat the West differently from the rest.
Even when the U.S. displays extreme political dysfunction, investors still treat it as a haven. In the summer of
2011, when the last debt-ceiling fight coincided with a U.S. credit-rating downgrade by Standard & Poor's ,
the 10-year Treasury yield fell sharply, finishing September at less than 2%.
In contrast, emerging markets such as India, Turkey and Brazil have seen their stock markets and currencies
battered this year as structural problems --including persistent current-account deficits that leave them reliant
on hot-money inflows -- have resurfaced. Any shutdown-related weakness in the U.S. would likely result in
slower trade, a headwind for many emerging economies since the financial crisis. It also would undercut
commodities prices -- oil fell Monday -- hurting big exporters of raw materials such as Brazil and Russia.
Having fallen for most of the year, emerging markets staged a rally in September. But this owed more to a
surprise delay in the U.S. Federal Reserve's tapering of bond purchases, pushing more investor dollars back
into higher-yielding assets.
This wrinkle does raise the possibility that any economic fallout from Washington's wrangles could be offset to
some degree by further Fed delays to reducing its bond-buying program.
But while that may help emerging markets at the margin, even a delay carries the implicit message that
tapering is coming at some point.
Moreover, the broader implication is that emerging markets' fates remain tied to decisions taken in a city that
is often criticized for not caring about bits of its own country, let alone the rest of the world.

Arts & Entertainment: Adapting Graphic Novel for the Stage
By Lizzie Simon

30 September 2013

English
Copyright 2013 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
When the playwright Lisa Kron set out in 2007 to adapt Alison Bechdel's "Fun Home" for the stage, Jeanine
Tesori was the only composer she had in mind.
The three women seemed particularly well matched: Ms. Bechdel's memoir in the form of a graphic novel
made nearly every "best of books" list when it was first published in 2006. By that point, Ms. Tesori had
composed music for "Caroline, or Change," "Thoroughly Modern Millie" and "Twelfth Night," earning Tony
nominations for each.
And Ms. Kron had transformed her own autobiographical material for the theater in the Obie award-winning
"2.5 Minute Ride," and in "Well," which was nominated for two Tony Awards when it ran on Broadway in
2006.
But for "Fun Home," opening Monday at the Public Theater, the discussion got off to a rocky start when Ms.
Tesori confessed that she had no idea how to make it into a musical.
"I was panicked," said Ms. Kron. "I felt my heart sink."
What vexed Ms. Tesori was that she had no experience adapting a graphic novel for the stage. Musicals,
even challenging ones, demand structural simplicity and a linear story line, whereas the graphic novel lays
out a series of cells containing illustrated moments, often with captions and bubbled dialogue, that can jump
time and setting from cell to cell.
Could a performance tell such a nonlinear story? "That's where we really lost our stomach linings," Ms. Tesori
said. But it was also what brought her on board, she added. "I knew it was going to sing."
"Fun Home" tells the story of Ms. Bechdel's childhood and adolescence in rural Pennsylvania in the 1970s.
Her father, the town funeral director and a high-school English teacher, is also a closeted gay man, who
eventually commits suicide by throwing himself into the path of a Sunbeam delivery truck.
As an adult, Alison comes out of the closet and takes her family's history head-on, unraveling the various lies
that her parents told her growing up.
"I grew up in that time," said Ms. Tesori. "We were being fed and sold the image of a TV family, and the
singing family in particular, that was so dissonant with what our experience was."
Not everyone would see a hit musical in a story about suicide, repressed sexuality and funeral homes, but
Ms. Kron said that it contains a timeless theme: a child yearning for intimacy with a parent.
"The book means so much to people," she said, "because the ache for that relationship is so palpable."
Ms. Kron and Ms. Tesori found their director, Sam Gold , when he overheard them discussing how to adapt
the book at the Ojai Playwrights Conference in 2009.
"I was immediately fascinated," he said. "We're used to telling stories where event causes action, which
causes another event, which causes a reaction, until there's a climax. But you can tell a story very powerfully
by saying here are six pieces of information, all living simultaneously. I thought: What an interesting theatrical
conversation."
The creative disagreements among the artists were many -- Ms. Tesori characterized them as "genteel but
ferocious." The process, she said, has been the most challenging, and most satisfying, of her career.
"There was no straight shot," said Ms. Kron, agreeing. "Just years of trial and error, trial and error, trial and
error."

Financial Markets: Investors Little Fazed by Capitol Theatrics
Financial Markets: Investors Little Fazed by Capitol Theatrics
By Katy Burne, Carolyn Cui and Kaitlyn Kiernan
803 words
28 September 2013
The Wall Street Journal
J
B5
English
Copyright 2013 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
As the clock winds down on a possible government shutdown and a potential U.S. debt default, Bernie
Williams has a simple answer for what he is doing to prepare.
"Nothing," said the chief investment officer at USAA Investment Management, which manages more than $57
billion in mutual-fund assets. "We have to just let the theater play out."
The comments underscore the irritation on Wall Street in the latest clash between Democrats and
Republicans in Washington. The U.S. Treasury warned Wednesday it could exhaust its borrowing capacity as
soon as the middle of next month, a development that analysts warned could force the government to choose
whether to continue to pay interest on U.S. government debt some time in subsequent weeks.
Yet signs of distress in financial markets are few and far between. The cost to insure for a year against a
default on 10 million euros ($13.5 million) of U.S. Treasurys rose sixfold over the past week to 34,500 euros
($46,530), but that is less than half the sum changing hands in the last debt-ceiling standoff in summer 2011.
The prices of one-month Treasury debt have fallen, but those of benchmark 10-year notes have risen, taking
the yield to 2.620% Friday, down from 3% earlier this month.
"It is fair to conclude from current rates that most view the theatrics in Washington as a movie they've seen
before," said Tony Crescenzi, executive vice president for Pacific Investment Management Co., Allianz SE 's
giant asset-management unit.
U.S. stocks fell Friday, but the Dow Jones Industrial Average remains just 2.7% below its all-time high. Two
traditional market-fear indicators, the CBOE Volatility Index and the gold price, are below their month-ago
levels. The dollar, which fell in past government-disruption episodes, was flat over the past week.
"The market is pretty complacent about this whole fiscal fight in Washington," said David Woo , head of global
rates and currencies research at Bank of America , head of global rates and currencies research at Merrill
Lynch .
To be sure, some investors are pulling back. U.S. money-market funds saw a net inflow of $27 billion in the
week ended Sept. 25, the biggest weekly inflow since late December, while over $7 billion flowed out of U.S.
equity funds, according to EPFR Global, a fund-data provider.
"There are some subdued concerns in the market, because many of the normal indicators of risk are not
flashing warning signs," said Edward Lashinski, director of global strategy and execution for RBC Capital
Markets ' futures group.
Still, Mr. Woo said he believes a complacent market is set up for a "pain trade." He expects the yen, gold and
Treasury inflation-protected securities, which pay buyers if inflation picks up, to rally if the U.S. growth
outlook sours and the Federal Reserve further delays paring back its monthly stimulus program. He said the
Washington drama could hit consumer confidence and slow the domestic recovery.
Some Bank of America Merrill Lynch clients bought options on Friday that confer the right to sell the dollar as
an insurance against the risk that the dollar will slide if the government is shut down and the debt ceiling isn't
raised.
Many other investors say they are buying on any substantial drop in stocks. "The way I see it, we're still in an
expansion mode for stocks, and a 5% to 10% drop is just one of those things you might have to put up with
along the way," said John Manley, chief equity strategist at Wells Fargo 's funds unit, which manages about
$200 billion in assets.
In part, that is because the global economic outlook appears rosier than it did in 2011.

--- Page 72 ---
Page 72 of 192 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Some say the bigger risk will come in the second half of October should the debt ceiling remain at its current
level, said Steven Hess, lead analyst for U.S. sovereign debt at Moody's Investors Service , which gives the
U.S. its highest triple-A rating.
He said an extended standoff eventually could force the government to decide which obligations it will honor.
Moody's believes interest payments on Treasury debt will be "one of their top priorities."
Hans Humes , chief executive of hedge fund Greylock Capital Management , which has $750 million of
assets under management, said his firm has 20% of its cash stockpiled in case of abnormal moves in
markets, far greater than its 7% cash allocation from a few months ago.
"We have seen so many crises, and we just assume it gets worked out, but this one could really ravage
confidence" if it spirals out of control, Mr. Humes said.

WEEKEND INVESTOR --- The Best Company Retirement Plans --- How much could a gold-plated plan mean to you? We found the most lucrative...
WEEKEND INVESTOR --- The Best Company Retirement Plans --- How much could a gold-plated plan
mean to you? We found the most lucrative offerings for employees in the U.S. Here's what you can
learn from them
By Liam Pleven
2,402 words
28 September 2013
The Wall Street Journal
J
B7
English
Copyright 2013 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Corrections & Amplifications
The first word of Vanguard Center for Retirement Research was misspelled as VanCguard in a Weekend
Investor article on Saturday about the best company retirement plans.
(WSJ Oct. 1, 2013)
(END)
Many Americans face a yawning gap between how much they have saved and how much they will need
when they leave the workforce.
But a handful of large companies give employees a big assist in the form of substantial contributions to their
retirement plans. The difference between the richest benefits and ordinary offerings for the average worker
often amounts to thousands of dollars a year, and sometimes tens of thousands.
That divide is largely hidden from public view, and many people know little about how their retirement plans
compare with others. So The Wall Street Journal asked BrightScope , a San Diego firm that rates retirement
plans, to calculate just how generous large plans offered by employers were, based on documents filed with
the federal government.
Among the findings: The most lucrative plans routinely provided the average worker $12,000 to $20,000 a
year to sock away, above and beyond their pay -- more than three to five times the average company
contribution in retirement plans of a similar size around the country, according to the analysis. One plan
topped $30,000.
The differences among plans could have a significant impact on when workers will be able to retire, and how
comfortably. Employers have been moving steadily away from traditional pensions, adding to the importance
for workers of having other sources of retirement income -- and of finding the best deal possible.
"There's a lot of variation, and you should know about the variation and exploit it," says Stephen Utkus, who
runs the VanCguard Center for Retirement Research.
At Deloitte, the accounting giant, a profit-sharing plan gave participants $30,806 a year on average in recent
years, according to the analysis. The plan is intended to help "attract and retain the highest caliber
professional talent," the firm said in a statement.
Plans covering pilots at major airlines also were among the richest, led by United Airlines ' plan, which gave
the average participant $23,476 annually. United merged with Continental Airlines in 2010, and is now known
as United Continental Holdings .
Financial firms also ranked high. Wellington Management , investment adviser on the mammoth Vanguard
Wellington Fund, gave workers in its retirement plan an average of $19,625 a year, according to the analysis.
General Re, a Berkshire Hathaway insurance unit, contributed $16,732 worth of Berkshire stock, on average.
Company contributions are in addition to whatever money employees set aside for retirement on their own,
either through employer-sponsored plans or independent accounts.
In many cases, the heftiest contributions represented well over the 10% to 12% of income that experts say is
the minimum workers might need to save to have a good chance of making it through retirement without
lowering their standard of living.

--- Page 74 ---
Page 74 of 192 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Capital Group Cos ., which runs American Funds, says it gives employees 15% of compensation -- resulting
in an average contribution of $15,801, according to the analysis. At United Continental, the airline contributes
16% to pilots' retirement accounts.
Pilots at American Airlines currently get 14%, rising to 16% in January if the merger between parent AMR and
US Airways Group closes by then, according to a person familiar with the matter. The retirement benefits are
less than pilots got before American sought bankruptcy protection, the person said, but the percentage is still
relatively high.
In a number of the most-lucrative plans, employees don't need to make any contribution to get company
money. Other firms make outright contributions and match what workers set aside.
Amgen , a biotechnology concern, says it provides 5% of pay automatically through its 401(k) plan, and
matches up to 5% of employee contributions -- which amounted to more than $13,000 a year in company
money for the average employee, according to the analysis.
In many cases, plans that provide the biggest contributions cover a workforce largely made up of highly
compensated employees. But there are exceptions.
W.W. Grainger , a supplier of industrial gear with many workers in sales and distribution-center operations,
sets aside up to 18% of compensation per year for employees, including those who are paid hourly,
according to company officials.
"They make no contribution to it, except hard work," says Joseph High, senior vice president for the Lake
Forest, Ill., firm. Workers in the plan got $10,347 on average a year from the company, according to the
analysis.
BrightScope conducted the analysis based on company contributions to employees enrolled in 479 401(k),
profit-sharing or other defined-contribution private-sector retirement plans, covering more than 11 million
workers, from 2007 to 2011, the most recent years for which data are filed with the U.S. Labor Department.
To be included, plans had to have at least $1 billion in assets in one of those five years. That leaves out many
smaller firms and professional partnerships that might offer richer benefits.
There are federal caps on annual contributions to these kinds of retirement plans, which allow workers to
defer taxes. Individuals can currently put in up to $17,500 a year, and combined contributions from individuals
and employers can add up to $51,000, according to Mark Luscombe , principal federal tax analyst at CCH, a
unit of Wolters Kluwer . Workers 50 years or older can put aside another $5,500. In addition, only $255,000 of
pay can be considered.
The analysis highlights large gaps in the amounts Americans are likely to have set aside when they retire.
The average employee in the 479 plans, offered by some of America's largest companies, received $3,863 a
year in the period.
In addition, less-lucrative plans often provide a lower percentage of pay. In defined-contribution plans that
offer a standard company match, the typical company match is worth 3% of pay and requires the employee to
contribute 6% to get that company money, according to Vanguard Group data on plans where it serves as
record-keeper.
About half the plans that let employees defer pay also offer employer contributions that aren't tied to what an
employee puts in, according to Vanguard. Those contributions typically equate to 4.2% of pay.
More broadly, fewer than half the workers in the private sector are covered by a retirement plan at work,
according to Alicia Munnell , director of the Center for Retirement Research at Boston College. "Enormous
disparities exist," she says.
The differences among retirement plans also show how a company's strategic goals shape workers' benefits.
Companies routinely look at what others in the industry offer, according to experts.
"Everybody is going to pay lip service to the idea that they're taking care of their employees," says Mike
Alfred , chief executive of BrightScope. In the boardroom, he adds, the considerations are "much more
Machiavellian."
Employees who understand the system can see how their employers and competitors stack up and weigh the
factors that might be driving benefit decisions -- and learn how to look for the best deal.
Here is what workers need to know:
Comparisons Can Be Tough

--- Page 75 ---
Page 75 of 192 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
It isn't always easy to determine whether one company's retirement benefits are richer than another's -- or
how those benefits fit into the bigger picture of overall compensation.
Companies file basic information with the Labor Department. BrightScope 's analysis for the Journal used
those filings to figure out which plans resulted in the most money going to the average employee.
That dollar figure is telling, experts say. Companies weigh how much they want to spend on benefits, and
employees care how much falls to their personal bottom line.
But that sum isn't the whole story. A company contribution worth tens of thousands of dollars might mean a
lot to someone making $60,000, but much less to someone earning $400,000, Ms. Munnell says.
"If you don't know the income of the people, it's very hard to say if that's a lot or a little," she says.
Experts look at what percentage of income retirement savings represent. But companies don't have to
disclose publicly what percentage of income they contribute, experts say. There is no public clearinghouse
where outsiders can get specific information, so finding out about a rival company or a potential employer
might require some digging or networking.
Corporate retirement benefits also sometimes include more than one plan, so employees could be receiving
company contributions in a 401(k) plan but also be due benefits through a separate defined-benefit plan, for
example.
A retirement plan also is one of many possible benefits. A particularly generous health plan, for instance,
could be worth more than a bigger company contribution to a 401(k).
Many industries also emphasize other types of pay. Wall Street, for instance, favors bonuses.
Lastly, even two retirement plans that provide similar company contributions can be more or less desirable for
employees, depending upon how good the mutual funds or other investment options are -- and the fees those
plans and funds charge.
Plans Have a Purpose
Retirement plans also can include a mix of outright contributions and matching contributions. Companies can
match employee contributions dollar for dollar, 50 cents on the dollar or in a variety of other ways.
"These plans have more angles than a box of ice cubes," says Jim Phillips, president of Retirement
Resources, a Peabody, Mass., firm that helps companies design and manage plans.
Remember one point amid the chaos -- the design is probably not an accident. Companies generally have a
strategy when they structure a plan.
Often, it is about keeping up with rivals. Philip de Toledo, president of Capital Group, says the company's aim
is "to be competitive with premier employers in all of the places where we work."
Competitors in the mutual-fund business also provide a significant percentage of pay. Fidelity Investments ,
for instance, matches employee contributions up to 7% of eligible compensation, and it puts up to another
10% aside as a profit-sharing contribution.
Companies contribute more when they have "unique labor pools that are highly skilled," where workers are
generally well-paid and generate high revenue per employee, according to BrightScope 's Mr. Alfred.
Offering a significant amount of deferred compensation through a retirement plan also can help create a
certain type of workforce, says Vanguard's Mr. Utkus. The thinking? "I'm attracting the long-term thinkers, the
patient employees," he says.
At Grainger, the industrial-supply firm, the percentage of compensation the company contributes is tied to
profitability, a common practice. The contributions also ramp up for longer-tenured workers, so that it is
calculated on 20% of pay for someone at the company for a year, but 100% of pay for someone at the
company for five years, Mr. High says.
The company makes no products of its own, it only supplies them -- so there is a premium on service, adds
Mr. High, whose other title is chief people officer. "We demand that you perform, and perform over time," he
says. "We have to deliver for customers."
Some companies also calibrate contributions to try to entice workers to set aside a certain amount of their
own money. Mr. Phillips says two investment firms he knows -- including a hedge fund with about 25

--- Page 76 ---
Page 76 of 192 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
employees -- match employee contributions dollar for dollar up to 10% of pay, with the company putting in
another 6% as well.
At Capital Group, Mr. de Toledo says helping workers save for a secure retirement decreases the chance
older employees will feel forced to keep punching the clock. "It doesn't have them stay here just for the
money," he says. "They're here because they want to be."
Plans Aren't Set in Stone
Americans who want higher Social Security benefits can lobby Congress, while workers who want a richer
retirement plan typically have to persuade their employer. Both are daunting challenges.
Employees who want to give it a try in the workplace should prepare a clear, strong argument backed up with
supporting evidence, says Chris Lyon , a partner at Rocaton Investment Advisors, a Norwalk, Conn., firm that
works with companies on their plans.
"That gets attention," he says. "If they're considering a change, it might push them over the fence." If a
number of employees express the same desire, that might also carry more weight, he says.
Workers can push for lower fees, different investment options or other changes. Asking the company to make
bigger contributions can be a tough argument, Mr. Lyon says.
But knowing the landscape can pay off in other ways. Job seekers, in particular, can study the different
benefits at prospective employers in order to get a sense of who the company is competing with, what kinds
of workers they want to attract -- and what the total compensation package might look like.
The flip side is that employers can sometimes cut retirement benefits, and some did amid the financial crisis.
Benefits are a major expense at many companies, and some look for ways to trim the cost. Plan documents
should spell out whether the employer has committed to any mandatory contributions, says CCH's Mr.
Luscombe.
Kevin Skow, a principal at Milliman, a consulting firm in Seattle, says one manufacturing firm he knows of
dropped its matching contribution, which amounted to 50% of what an employee set aside up to 6% of pay, in
2009. The company reinstated the match in 2011, but at a lower level, he says.
The issue for a company that is cutting back: "What is the point at which participants are going to start
reacting negatively?" Mr. Skow says.
As the economy has improved, some companies have reinstated contributions they had pared back, Mr. Lyon
says. But there still is slack in the labor market and inflation remains low, making it easier for companies to
hold the line against richer benefits.
"They are inching back to the pre-financial-crisis generosity. But they are not there yet," Mr. Lyon says. As for
when they might make it all the way back, he adds, "Not quickly."

Stocks rose, breaking the S&P 500 out a five-day slump that was its longest losing streak this year.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average advanced 55.04 points, or 0.4%, to close at 15328.30. Thursday's
advance was the first since the Dow industrials hit a record high last week, and came on quiet day of trading
that investors said brought little market-moving news. The blue chips had lost 404 points, or 2.6% over the

The S&P 500 tacked on 5.90 points, or 0.3%, to 1698.67, snapping its own five-session slide. The Nasdaq

telecommunications stocks.
Uncertainty over the Federal Reserve's policy intentions and rising tension in Washington surrounding budget
and debt-ceiling talks have ushered anxiety into the stock market in recent days.
On Thursday, lawmakers were grappling over a short-term spending bill to keep the government running
when the next fiscal year starts Oct. 1.
"These kind of movements in the short term are typical given the noise we're getting," he said. "It's pretty
normal to see a pause in the market. People are getting set, looking toward what's going to happen when
third-quarter earnings start in October."
The Russell 2000 index of small-capitalization stocks has climbed 6.7% this month, while growth-sensitive

17% so far this year.

pessimism about the market's future direction.
"We hit all-time highs five days ago, so it's logical that the market is going to take a pause here," said Frank
Ingarra, head trader at NorthCoast Asset Management.

Stocks Extend Declines
Monday's Markets
Stocks Extend Declines
By Alexandra Scaggs
653 words
24 September 2013
The Wall Street Journal
J
C4
English
Copyright 2013 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Stocks fell for a third consecutive session as investors grappled with an uncertain outlook for Federal
Reserve policy.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average slipped 49.71 points, or 0.3%, to 15401.38. The S&P 500 index lost 8.07
points, or 0.5%, to 1701.84. The Nasdaq Composite Index lost 9.44 points, or 0.3%, to 3765.29.
Stocks rallied last Wednesday after the Fed surprised investors with a decision to keep its bond-buying
program intact, rather than trim some of its purchases of Treasurys as expected.
But since then, both the Dow industrials and S&P 500 have shed all of those gains. Since Wednesday, when
both benchmarks closed at records, the Dow has fallen 1.8% and the S&P 500 has lost 1.4%. Still, they are
respectively up 18% and 19% for the year.
The Dow had undone its gains by Friday's close, and the S&P 500 closed below its prerally levels Monday.
Despite the decision from the Fed last week, "people are thinking that not much has changed after all," said
Art Steinmetz , chief investment officer with OppenheimerFunds, which manages $209 billion.
Traders said Monday's selling was subdued compared with Friday's steep declines.
Jonathan Corpina , senior managing partner with brokerage firm Meridian Equity Partners Inc ., said that he
didn't see investors rushing for the exits. Instead, he said, they were "trying to figure out where we are" after
the Fed's decision threw markets for a loop.
Investors "weren't positioned accordingly," Mr. Corpina said. "People are [saying], 'wait a second, we might
have overreacted here'" by buying into Wednesday's rally.
Linda Duessel , equity market strategist at Federated Investors, which manages $384 billion, said that she
expects economic growth to pick up at the end of this year and into next year, which should mean good
things for the stock market.
"We're really bullish as we make our way into next year," she said. And with "the Fed's decision to keep the
money free and easy . . . it's all-clear for stocks."
One lingering concern for investors is the possibility of nasty budget negotiations in Congress and a
government shutdown.
Ms. Duessel thinks the S&P 500 could see declines between 5% and 7% over the next month as the debate
picks up. But the firm expects the index to end the year between 1700 and 1750, which would mean, at most,
a 2.8% gain from current levels.

Taper Delay Boosts British Equities --- The U.K.'s Fortunes Are Linked to Emerging Markets, Which Benefit From Fed's Easy-Money Policies
Taper Delay Boosts British Equities --- The U.K.'s Fortunes Are Linked to Emerging Markets, Which
Benefit From Fed's Easy-Money Policies
By Nina Bains
800 words
24 September 2013
The Wall Street Journal
J
C4
English
Copyright 2013 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Some investors are betting the momentum that has carried U.S. stocks to record highs is shifting across the
Atlantic.
The FTSE 100, the benchmark index for U.K. stocks, is up 11% this year, trailing the S&P 500, which has
gained 19%.
U.K. shares have performed better lately, after the Federal Reserve's decision last week to keep up its
monthly bond purchases. The Fed's easy-money policies are particularly helpful for the U.K. because many
listed companies have a strong presence in emerging markets, which benefit as the Fed's move emboldens
investors to take on risk.
"The continued easy money will ease the pressure on emerging-market currencies and could fuel a rally in
these stocks and sectors, which would see the U.K. market do well," said Scott Meech, head of European
equities at UBP Investment Management, which has 330 million euros ($446 million) in assets.
British stocks may be poised to close the gap with their U.S. counterparts even further, as economic growth
takes root in the U.K., even as the U.S. recovery shows signs of faltering. For the first time since 2002, more
fund managers are bullish than bearish on U.K. stocks, according to a survey by Bank of America-Merrill
Lynch. Investors have put $1.4 billion into funds that buy U.K. shares this year, including nearly $400 million
in the week ended Sept. 18, according to data provider EPFR Global.
The U.K. upswing involves more than stocks. The pound has rallied 3.5% this month, as the strengthening
economy heightens expectations the Bank of England will raise interest rates sooner than anticipated.
Higher rates boost the appeal of owning U.K. debt.
The U.K. economy has improved rapidly since the start of this year, when it narrowly avoided its third
recession in five years. On Wednesday, the Bank of England raised its forecast for growth in the third quarter
from the previous period to 0.7% from 0.5%.
In contrast to that is U.S. jobs data that have disappointed in the last two months. Moreover, a budget battle
getting under way in Congress adds new risks, analysts say. The Organization for Economic Cooperation and
Development said recent indicators point to growth in the U.K. outpacing expansion by the U.S., Germany
and Japan in the second half of 2013.
The strong recent performance of U.K. equities is entirely attributable to solid economic data, said Scott
Jamieson, head of multiasset investing at Kames Capital in London, with $24 billion under management. "It's
hard to remember the last time a data item disappointed."
Mr. Jamieson has been loading up on U.K. stocks since the spring, particularly in consumer-goods firms,
anticipating that faster growth will lead to more retail spending. Retailers in the FTSE 350 index are up 36%
this year. On Monday, the FTSE 100 ended down 0.6% at 6557.37.
The Fed's bond buying holds down yields in the U.S., and makes it cheap for investors to borrow to buy
assets that promise better returns, including emerging-markets stocks and gold. Additionally, a pickup in
China's industrial sector is helping mining and energy stocks, which make up 25% of the weighting in the
FTSE 100.
The winners since last week's Fed meeting include Randgold Resources Ltd ., which mines gold in Africa,
and Aberdeen Asset Management , an investment fund that specializes in emerging markets. They have
risen 3% and 6%, respectively.

--- Page 83 ---
Page 83 of 192 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
UBP's Mr. Meech has increased his holdings of U.K. equities for most of this year. Dixons Retail PLC , an
electronics retailer, and Barratt Developments PLC , a property company, are among the stocks that could
benefit as the U.K. economy improves, Mr. Meech said.
U.K. stocks are cheaper relative to their U.S. counterparts. The average forward-looking price/earnings ratio
in the FTSE 100 is 12.98, while it is 15.41 for the S&P 500, according to Birinyi Associates.
To be sure, the Fed still is expected to reduce its easing in the coming months, which could drain some cash
from stocks. And some investors argue that the U.K.'s improving economy is reflected in share prices already.
"The easy money has been made," said Edward Legget, manager of the Standard Life Investments U.K.
Equity Unconstrained Fund, who manages about GBP 1 billion ($1.6 billion). He has sold some of his U.K.
stocks this year, and says consumer stocks in particular don't have much room to rise.

Investing: Financial Advisers Shift After Surprise
Investing: Financial Advisers Shift After Surprise
By Daisy Maxey

20 September 2013
The Wall Street Journal

C4
English
Copyright 2013 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
The Federal Reserve's surprise decision not to trim its bond-buying program caught many financial advisers
off guard. Many of them are now recalibrating their advice for clients.
Some are hedging clients' fixed-income portfolios or moving them into bonds with less interest-rate risk while
others are strategically adding more stocks.
Markets rallied after Fed Chairman Ben Bernanke said Wednesday that the central bank didn't have a fixed
schedule to end bond purchases -- part of its so-called quantitative-easing program -- despite earlier
expectations that it would.
Nicholas LaVerghetta, principal at NCM Capital Management in Ramsey, N.J., was a bit surprised by the
Fed's decision. He had told his clients about three weeks ago that while recent economic data didn't support
a significant tapering, he expected a very small one.
Over the past few months, Mr. LaVerghetta had trimmed clients' bond exposure, and now he plans to reduce
it further. "I expect rates to head back up in a matter of months," he says.
Mr. LaVerghetta, whose firm manages $80 million, has been hedging against rising interest rates by
investing in the MetWest Unconstrained Bond Fund and the Driehaus Select Credit Fund. In the wake of the
Fed's statement, he is considering adding inverse funds or options as further hedges.
He also has been adding more European and frontier-market stocks to clients' portfolios over the past few
months and plans to continue building those positions.
After the Fed's announcement, "I think stocks are much more attractive than bonds," he says.
Jeff Seymour, managing director at Triangle Wealth Management LLC in Cary, N.C., in his weekly newsletter
to clients Tuesday predicted the Fed would trim its $85 billion in monthly purchases to $75 billion, and then
take no further action until next spring or summer.
But after the Fed's statements, he sent an email to his clients saying he will be making some minor
adjustments over the coming days and weeks.
He expects over the next few days or weeks to put 5% to 8% of his clients' portfolios in exchange-traded
funds that invest in gold. The portfolios currently hold no gold, but their exposure has fluctuated from zero to
as high as 10% this year.
He also anticipates the S&P 500 will fall from Thursday's finish of 1722.34 to near 1690 by the first week of
October, at which time he plans to do some tactical buying, perhaps via the Vanguard S&P 500
exchange-traded fund or a small-cap growth ETF, he says.
John Gugle, an adviser at Alpha Financial Advisors in Charlotte, N.C., was taken aback by the Fed's
announcement, but hasn't yet decided what to say to clients. He expected the Fed to lay out its plan for
cutting and had talked to clients at length about why the talk of a stimulus cutback was a good sign on the
economy's health, he says.
"It scares me that they're not going to do the tapering because maybe they're seeing something that the
market hasn't priced in," he says.
Still, he suspects his clients are taking a very positive view. "I think clients saw the market's rally and thought
everything's fine," he says.

--- Page 85 ---
Page 85 of 192 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
The Fed may be hoping to motivate investors to move back into bonds in the belief that quantitative easing is
still going strong and that rates will fall, Mr. Gugle says. But he doesn't see 10-year Treasury rates, which
ended Thursday's session at 2.748%, moving back to 1.6%.
He says he plans to stay put on the shorter end of the yield curve, and doesn't have any plans to add to his
stock position for now.
Mimi Lord, chief investment officer at Spero-Smith Investment Advisers Inc. in Cleveland, told her clients in a
July letter that "the time for less monetary intervention is upon us."
But the Fed's decision has made Ms. Lord's firm even more positive about adding the Goldman Sachs
Strategic Income Fund to client portfolios, something it has been considering since early this year and
approved just days before the Fed spoke, Ms. Lord says.

Thursday's Markets
Surprise Reverts To Unease
By Alexandra Scaggs, Chris Dieterich and Dan Strumpf
822 words

The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2013 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
A day after stocks surged to new highs on the Federal Reserve's decision to stand pat on monetary stimulus,
some investors were having second thoughts.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average fell 40.39 points, or 0.3%, to 15636.55. Many traders said the pullback on
Thursday showed how investors were plotting their next moves rather than rushing to buy stocks with the
Dow up nearly 6% in September and 19% for the year.
"The Fed's U-turn [Wednesday] caught a lot of investors off guard," said Jeff Schwarte, a Des Moines,
Iowa-based stock portfolio manager at Principal Global Investors , which manages $292 billion.
Some investors said the Fed's move wouldn't prevent the U.S. central bank from ultimately stepping back its
support of financial markets. Others highlighted signs of economic softness and slowing earnings growth. A
few were eyeing budget debates in Congress that already are looking contentious.
"The market hasn't contemplated exactly why the Fed did not start" to withdraw its stimulus, said Kristina
Hooper, U.S. investment strategist for Allianz Global Investors, which oversees $409 billion.
To be sure, investors say stocks still have a better outlook than bonds, which could put extra wind in the sails
of stocks.
"The relative return prospects for stocks look measurably better than bonds," said Jim McDonald , chief
investment strategist at Northern Trust, which bought stocks last week and manages $803 billion. "You really
need to become bearish on growth to like the bond market here."
But since earnings growth hasn't revved up, stocks are starting to look pricier to some skeptics. The stocks in
the S&P 500-stock index trade at an average of 14.6 times analysts' estimates of their coming-year profit.
That is up from 12.6 at the end of 2012. Wall Street analysts expect 6.1% yearly earnings growth for
companies in the S&P 500, a pace some say doesn't justify the roughly 20% advance in blue-chip stocks this
year.
"It's debatable how rapidly profits can grow," said Hank Herrmann , CEO of Waddell & Reed Financial , an
asset manager that oversees $104 billion.
Investors' wariness was apparent in the week ended Sept. 4, when they pulled money from mutual funds for
the first time since June, according to data from fund-tracker Lipper.
In the two weeks since, mutual-fund inflows, traditionally the realm of long-term investors, have been
outpaced by a flood of cash into exchange-traded funds, often used by faster-moving investors. ETF
investors jumped back into the stock market in the past two weeks, sending a total $18 billion into U.S.
stocks, compared with mutual funds' $2.4 billion.
To be sure, some of the flows may have been driven by fears about September tapering that have since
dissipated.
Even so, much of Wednesday's trading volume at Bank of AmericaMerrill Lynch was in ETFs, said Doug
Crofton, managing director in equities trading with the bank. Because of that, he said, it wasn't yet clear
whether the buying would turn into long-term bets from investors.
Fewer stocks listed on the New York Stock Exchange are hitting yearly highs than the last time the market
was pushing into record territory, according to FactSet. On Aug. 1, 386 NYSE-listed stocks reached new
yearly highs, compared with 313 during Wednesday's rally.

beneficiaries of Wednesday's 147-point Dow rally after the Fed's decision to continue buying $85 billion of
bonds monthly increased the shares' appeal relative to bonds. Shares of home builders also slumped after

European markets rallied. The Stoxx Europe 600 gained 0.6% as investors shrugged off data showing that
U.K. retail sales for August fell unexpectedly. The U.K.'s FTSE 100 index climbed 1%, while Germany's DAX
advanced 0.7% to close at a record.
Treasury bonds pulled back Thursday as investors cashed out some chips after the biggest rally since

Fed's Surprise Powers Market Surge --- DJIA Rises 147.21, Bonds, Gold Jump As 'Taper' Comes Off

By Alexandra Scaggs, Min Zeng and Chris Dieterich

English

"Where's the taper?" Like millions of investors around the world, Thomas Urano was stunned by the Federal
Reserve on Wednesday.
Mr. Urano, a fixed-income portfolio manager at Sage Advisory Services , which manages $10 billion, soon got
his answer: there was no taper. Defying common market wisdom, the Fed decided to keep its bond-purchase
stimulus plan intact.
The market took the news very well. Stocks soared to new highs, while U.S. Treasury bonds posted the
biggest one-day price rally since November 2011, with the benchmark 10-year yield closing at 2.706%. Yields
move inversely to prices. Mortgage-backed securities, which are directly affected by the Fed program, leapt
even more.
Many commodities also gained, with gold futures jumping 4.5% in late trading, while the dollar sank against
the yen and euro, reflecting reduced investor expectations for U.S. interest-rate increases.
"The phones lit up like a pinball machine," said Art Hogan , managing director at Lazard Capital Markets.
"We're not used to being surprised by Fed meetings. In the first half hour, we talked to at least 20 investors,
and everyone felt the same way."
The Dow Jones Industrial Average gained 147.21 points, or 0.95%, to 15676.94, an all-time closing high. The
S&P 500 index rose 20.76 points, or 1.2%, to a record 1725.52. The Nasdaq Composite Index rose 37.94
points, or 1%, to 3783.64.
With Wednesday's rally, the S&P is up 21% so far in 2013.
The gains added to a strong advance in September and came even as Wall Street wrestled with new
questions about the strength of the U.S. recovery. The Federal Reserve cut its U.S. growth forecasts for 2013
and 2014.
Among the best performers Wednesday were utilities and housing stocks, areas that benefit from continued
low interest rates. Also gaining were overseas issues, particularly in Latin America, and the shares of
companies dealing in raw materials.
"It's a big shock, and it's a massive green light for a risk-on party," said Benoit Anne, global head of
emerging-market strategy at Societe Generale .
For months, many investors had been looking to this week's meeting of the Fed's policy-making committee as
a likely turning point in the Fed's easy-money policies, which have played a major role in keeping interest
rates low and fueling the stock market's rally. Most investors had expected the Fed to pull back on its
monthly bond purchases -- currently $85 billion -- by between $10 billion and $15 billion.
But Fed officials said they wanted more evidence of sustained improvement before making any moves.
"I'm surprised they didn't start to reduce bond purchases by a small amount," said Kate Warne, investment
strategist with Edward Jones. "We figured they'd take a baby step rather than nothing at all."
Still, investors noted the decision by the Fed to maintain its so-called quantitative easing efforts only
postpones the inevitable pullback by the central bank. The Fed's next policy-making meeting is scheduled
for the end of next month, and Fed Chairman Ben Bernanke said at his press conference Wednesday that a
paring of stimulus could take place this year.

--- Page 90 ---
Page 90 of 192 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
"Now we're back to this, where every [economic] report we're going to have to parse to see what the Fed
thinks," said Marc Doss , San Diego-based regional chief investment officer with Wells Fargo Private Bank.
For now, however, investors said the Fed's decision gives stocks the green light to push still further into
record-high territory. "You've got a trend that's up and a Fed that's in your corner, and you don't want to fight
the Fed," said Rob Glownia, quantitative analyst at RiverFront Investment Group LLC , in Richmond, Va.
A.C. Morgan, managing director in equities trading at UBS AG , said he saw buying by a diverse set of
investors after the statement and noted several of the big-picture issues that have troubled investors of late
have fallen by the wayside. "Syria, that's gone. Who's going to replace Bernanke? That's gone," he said. "And
what's tapering going to look like? We don't have to worry about that right now."
In the bond market, Wednesday's rally extended the benchmark 10-year note's winning streak into a sixth
consecutive session. The buying sent the yield to the lowest level in more than a month. Bond prices rise
when their yields fall.
The benchmark 10-year note yield fell to as low as 2.671% during the afternoon trade, the lowest level since
Aug. 13.
Traders and investors that placed bearish bets on bond prices have scrambled to cover their sour bets after
Lawrence Summers's announcement over the weekend that he was withdrawing from consideration to
succeed Mr. Bernanke. Wednesday's Fed statement prompted such bets to unwind further, traders said.
"The market was caught off guard" with the Fed's decision to stand pat, said Sean Simko , head of
fixed-income portfolio management at SEI Investments . "At this point the 10-year yield should be capped at
3%."

Fed's Surprise Powers Market Surge: Higher-Yielding Shares Are Seen As Benefiting Most From Status Quo
Fed's Surprise Powers Market Surge: Higher-Yielding Shares Are Seen As Benefiting Most From
Status Quo
By Alexandra Scaggs, Dan Strumpf and Chris Dieterich
761 words
19 September 2013
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2013 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
The Federal Reserve's decision to maintain its $85 billion-a-month bond-buying program breathed new life
into corners of the stock market that stand to benefit the most from rock-bottom interest rates.
Higher-yielding stocks such as utilities and real-estate investment trusts, or REITs, which investors favor in a
low-rate environment, led the market's advance Wednesday. Other beneficiaries included home builders,
which get a boost from the Fed's support of the housing market, and emerging-market shares, which stand to
gain from a weaker U.S. dollar.
By the same token, financials and health care are likely to be hurt, as the move will squeeze banks' income
and reduce investor interest in slower-expanding, more income-oriented stocks, known as defensive sectors.
Many of Wednesday's gainers were in favor in the early months of 2013, only to take a hit in May when Fed
officials began suggesting that the central bank could pull back on its attempts to stimulate the economy by
the end of this year.
Now, the strategy of buying higher-yielding stocks such as overseas companies, utilities and REITs is back in
play, at least for the time being.
"Assuming that we like the fundamentals in our interest-rate-sensitive names, I would probably actually be
looking to add to those," said John Jares, assistant vice president of equity investments at USAA .
REITs, which are a common destination for yield-hungry investors because they must pay out 90% of their
income to shareholders, staged strong gains Wednesday. The MSCI U.S. REIT Index rose 3.4%. Through
mid-August, the index had fallen as much as 18% from May 21, the day before Fed Chairman Ben Bernanke
first raised the prospect of scaling back the bond-buying program.
Utilities, too, jumped Wednesday. The utilities sector in the S&P 500-stock index gained 3%.
At the same time, the Fed's continuation of its easy-money policies is also seen brightening the outlook for
riskier corners of the stock market, especially those that would benefit from an improving global economy.
"If the Fed's going to provide stimulus, you might want to be in some of the aggressive sectors," such as
materials and technology shares, said Kate Warne, investment strategist with Edward Jones. "If you expect
the economy to improve, you'd expect them to be the beneficiaries."
Housing stocks are seen as getting a reprieve after taking a drubbing recently amid a rise in long-term rates.
The Dow Jones U.S. Select Home Builders Index jumped 4.6% Wednesday.
"It's like the Fed has taken the housing market and said, 'We've got your back,'" said Don Rissmiller, chief
economist at Strategas Research Partners. "They've created some tailwind, saying housing is very important
to this recovery and that they are going to structure policies so that financial conditions don't tighten too
quickly."
To be sure, the Fed will pare back its bond-buying efforts eventually. So investors piling into sectors that
benefit from a low-interest-rate environment risk facing a repeat of the group's swoon earlier this year, when it
appeared the Fed would start scaling back easing efforts soon.
Still, the Fed's decision not to taper its bond-buying program also is seen as a boon to emerging markets.
Stocks and currencies of such developing countries have been battered as the recent rise in interest rates
has prompted investors to return money to the developed world. But maintaining the bond-buying program
boosts the case for developing economies again by weakening the dollar and reining in U.S. yields, said
David Waddell, head of Waddell & Associates, which oversees about $700 million in Memphis, Tenn. The

--- Page 92 ---
Page 92 of 192 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
dollar fell against the euro, yen and most other currencies on Wednesday. "I think the real opportunity is in
the offshore [stocks] because this is a weak-dollar policy," Mr. Waddell said. "It's a clear indication to put
those emerging-market positions back on."
Meanwhile, financials are seen as laggards. Bank stocks had been among the best-performing sectors in
recent months because financials are seen as benefiting from higher long-term interest rates. The S&P 500
financial sector gained 1% Wednesday.

Markets Calm Before Fed Decision --- Investors Remain in Buying Mood Even Ahead of Anticipated Reduction of Bond-Buying Program
Markets Calm Before Fed Decision --- Investors Remain in Buying Mood Even Ahead of Anticipated
Reduction of Bond-Buying Program
By Mike Cherney, Carolyn Cui and Min Zeng
871 words
18 September 2013
The Wall Street Journal
J
C4
English
Copyright 2013 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
If investors are concerned about the imminent end to the Federal Reserve's bond-buying program, the
markets haven't noticed.
Despite widespread expectations that the Fed will announce a trimming of its asset purchases aimed at
pushing down interest rates and propping up the economic recovery, fund managers have been in a buying
mood lately.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average on Tuesday advanced for the 11th time in 14 trading sessions, and
Treasury prices rose for the fifth straight day.
The Dow rose 34.95, or 0.23%, to 15529.73. The S&P 500 index rose 7.16, or 0.42%, to 1704.76. The
Nasdaq Composite Index rose 27.85, or 0.75%, to 3745.70.
Many investors expect the Fed to decide to cut its $85 billion monthly purchases of bonds by about $10 billion
to $15 billion. Markets were roiled in May and June after Fed Chairman Ben Bernanke said the U.S. central
bank would consider reducing purchases in a process dubbed "tapering."
Some investors dismiss the prospect of much turbulence this time. They say players in stock, bond and
commodity markets have had time to prepare for potential Fed action, assuming the Fed acts largely within
market expectations, and that a selloff in bonds since Mr. Bernanke's comments means there is less potential
for a large price decline now.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average has risen 4.9% this month to within 1% of a record, while the yield on the
10-year Treasury note has held steady at about 2.85%. The Chicago Board Options Exchange's Volatility
Index, a measure of how much investors expect to see the S&P 500-stock index swing over the coming
month, was at 14.53 on Tuesday, in line with its 2013 average and below its long-term average near 20.
"They've been talking about tapering going back several months now," said Alex Roever, head of U.S.
interest-rate strategy for J.P. Morgan Securities. "So we sort of think the whole idea of tapering is priced into
the market."
Bond investors have been buying Treasury debt, reversing recent months of selling. Net speculative short
positions in Treasury futures rose to 688,000 contracts as of Sept. 10, the most bearish position since 2005,
according to data compiled by RBC Capital Markets . Short sellers have been buying bonds to close out
these bets over the past several days.
Scott Carmack , co-portfolio manager at Leader Capital, which oversees about $700 million in fixed-income
assets, said bond markets could rally Wednesday should the Fed taper within market expectations.
To be sure, large market swings are possible, particularly as traders digest the news following its release.
Outsize moves are more likely if the Fed decides to reduce its monthly purchases by more than $10 billion to
$15 billion, investors said.
Many investors also say stocks and bonds could be in for a selloff should the Fed signal it has decided to set
a steady course in winding down its bond-buying efforts. The extent of any decline, they say, would depend
on the degree to which the economy shows it is healthy enough to stand on its own.
Much of the concern stems from stock selloffs that arose out of previous Fed efforts to pull back on stimulus.
Stock prices tumbled by double-digit percentages after the first two rounds of Fed stimulus ended in 2010
and 2011. In the most recent episode, the yield on 10-year Treasury notes brushed over 3% earlier this
month -- the first time it crossed that mark in more than two years -- from 1.6% in May. When interest rates
go up, bond prices go down.

--- Page 94 ---
Page 94 of 192 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
But many investors now say bonds look like a buy. Ray Remy, head of fixed-income trading in New York at
Daiwa Capital Markets America Inc ., is buying Treasurys. He said he expects the 10-year yield to drop to
2.75%. "I am a buyer here, and I think the rise in yields in the past few months is overdone,"" said Mr. Remy.
In other markets, many investors are saying the tepid global economic picture is likely to reduce investors'
interest in the details of the Fed move.
Sean Corrigan, chief investment strategist at Switzerland-based Diapason Commodities Management, which

production shortages from Libya. Crude oil on the New York Mercantile Exchange , off 1.1% Tuesday, to

Gold has tumbled 22% this year amid signs of slowing stimulus from central banks. Copper, viewed as an

Several investment-grade companies sold bonds this week, taking advantage of the relative market calm
ahead of the Fed announcement. On Monday, ING Bank NV sold $2 billion in corporate bonds, and
consumer-goods company Reckitt Benckiser Group PLC sold $1 billion through a subsidiary.

Stocks Welcome Summers Retreat --- Move by A Candidate to Lead Fed Is Seen as a Boost for Easy-Money Policies
Monday's Markets
Stocks Welcome Summers Retreat --- Move by A Candidate to Lead Fed Is Seen as a Boost for
Easy-Money Policies
By Dan Strumpf, Min Zeng and Alexandra Scaggs
891 words
17 September 2013
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2013 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Stocks and bonds staged a world-wide rally as investors took news that Lawrence Summers had withdrawn
from the contest to lead the Federal Reserve as a sign of continued easy-money policies.
After a rally in Asian and European stocks overnight on the news, U.S. stocks leapt higher at the opening bell.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average climbed 118.72 points, or 0.8%, to 15494.78.
The S&P 500 index gained 9.61 points, or 0.6%, to 1697.60, and the Nasdaq Composite Index slipped 4.34
points, or 0.1%, to 3717.85.
The Dow is up 18% so far this year, while the S&P 500 is up 19% and the Nasdaq has gained 23%.
News of Mr. Summers's withdrawal also stoked a rise in U.S. Treasury prices and gold, but led the dollar
lower.
At its session highs, the Dow industrials were up as many as 174 points.
A midafternoon technical glitch that temporarily shut down stock-options trading may have contributed to the
market's pullback from the highs, traders said.
With investors viewing the U.S. economy and financial markets as heavily reliant on low interest rates, the
question of who will be the next Fed chairman has gotten more attention than is generally the case.
The next chief of the Fed is expected to oversee an unwinding of its $85 billion-a-month bond-buying
program, an effort that hasn't just kept interest rates low, but also helped fuel record highs in the stock
market and wide swings in the currency markets.
While Ben Bernanke 's term doesn't expire until January, the fate of the Fed's easing efforts is front and
center for investors this week. Fed officials are gathering for a two-day meeting beginning Tuesday at which
many in the markets expect the central bank to take its first step toward a "tapering" of those bond-market
purchases.
Given "unprecedented Fed activism" driving markets, "the changing of the guard . . . is naturally going to
drive attention, said Adrian Miller, director of global markets strategy at GMP Securities .
While the market response Monday may have been "overdone," Mr. Miller said, "strong reaction only confirms
the market's overall level of angst regarding the Fed's near and medium-term monetary policies."
Fueling the global rally were expectations that whoever now succeeds Mr. Bernanke as chairman will
continue the present course of monetary policy in the U.S., and that any scaling back of the central bank's
aggressive steps to stimulate the economy will be slow and gradual.
"It gives more certainty to the stimulus that's in place in the sense that it's not going to be taken away as
quickly as some might fear," said Reed Choate, portfolio manager at Neville, Rodie & Shaw, which oversees
$1.3 billion.
Many investors believed that Mr. Summers, a former Treasury secretary who has been one of President
Barack Obama's top economic advisers, would be more aggressive in reining in the Fed's bond-purchase
program.

--- Page 97 ---
Page 97 of 192 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
"The market needs and wants a commitment that the policies that Bernanke started will remain after he
departs," said Paul Zemsky , chief investment officer of multiasset strategies at ING Investment Management,
which oversees $180 billion. "With Summers, you weren't sure that would happen."
With the exit of Mr. Summers, many investors see Janet Yellen , the Fed's current vice chairwoman, as the
likely top contender for the post.
"The presumption is now Yellen is the nominee, so basically you're going to have Bernanke Two, which
would be very helpful for the financial markets," Mr. Zemsky said. "Now you're taking this risk away, you get
this relief rally."
In Europe, the DAX gained 1.22% to a record high.
In Asian trading, the Hang Seng Index gained 1.47%.
The bond market, too, experienced a relief rally Monday, traders said, although gains were pared in afternoon
trading as some investors booked profits from the market's four-session rally, traders said.
In late-afternoon trade, the benchmark 10-year Treasury note rose 6/32 in price, yielding 2.875%. When bond
prices rise, their yields fall.
Some wondered if Monday's rallies would continue, especially with the Fed still widely expected to begin
paring back on easing this week.
Fed officials are slated to announce their decision Wednesday afternoon, followed by a news conference by
Mr. Bernanke.
Meanwhile, currencies that are expected to benefit from continued easy monetary policy in the U.S. rallied
on news of Mr. Summers' withdrawal, while the dollar retreated.

slipping against other emerging-market currencies. The dollar also weakened against the euro and the yen.
Early Tuesday in Asia, stock markets were mixed. Japan's Nikkei was up 0.2%. Australia's S&P/ASX 200 was
down 0.2% and South Korea's Kospi was down 0.8%. The Shanghai composite opened flat.

Gold futures fell to their lowest price in five weeks as investors anticipated the Federal Reserve's coming

On Friday, gold for September delivery lost $22, or 1.7%, to end at $1,308.40 a troy ounce on the Comex

the week.
The Federal Open Market Committee meets Tuesday and Wednesday. While the Fed has said it would
eventually reduce its $85 billion-a-month bond purchases that support the economy, it hasn't yet given a firm
timeline.
"The market has come around to the concept that the Fed is going to announce some stimulus tapering," said
Frank McGhee, a senior precious-metals dealer with Integrated Brokerage Services Inc. in Chicago.
The Fed's bond purchases have helped keep interest rates low, allowing gold, a zero-yielding asset, to
more easily compete with interest-bearing assets like bonds. Ending the bond-buying program could send
investors out of gold and into other assets.
However, some market watchers say the Fed is unlikely to act in the wake of early September's disappointing
employment report. The Labor Department data showed that in recent months, the pace of hiring by private
employers has been slower than previously thought.
"How could they cut stimulus when they've said time and time again that their decision is tied to
employment?" said Bob Haberkorn , a senior commodities broker with RJO Futures in Chicago.
Gold's decline this past week also came as the potential for a U.S.-led military strike against Syria eased.
The U.S. and Russia have begun talks on how Syria would surrender control of its chemical weapons to
international authorities, decreasing the chance of military action that could lead to further instability in the
Mideast. Investors often buy gold in times of geopolitical uncertainty.
"There was much more of a Syria premium built into gold's rally than a lot of people expected," Mr. McGhee
said.
In other commodity markets, corn prices tumbled 6% a day after government forecasters projected a bigger
harvest this autumn than analysts had expected. Chicago Board of Trade corn for September delivery sank
29 cents to $4.50 a bushel.

Stock Markets: Stocks Waver on What Is 'Good' or 'Bad'
Stock Markets: Stocks Waver on What Is 'Good' or 'Bad'
By Tomi Kilgore
714 words
14 September 2013
The Wall Street Journal
J
B5
English
Copyright 2013 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
For the past several months, the stock market has been afflicted with a degree of schizophrenia. On some
days, weak news on the economy is good for stocks. On other days, it is strong readings on the economy that
give stocks a boost.
Underlying this odd dynamic have been tensions over the fate of the Federal Reserve's $85 billion-a-month
bond-buying stimulus program. In some cases, investors believe that better news on the economy will prompt
the Fed to pull back, taking away easy money that has supported the stock market since the financial crisis.
In others, investors say moderate growth in an environment of low interest rates is good for corporate profits
and the stock market -- even without special accommodation from the Fed.
"It's annoyingly inconsistent," Dan Greenhaus , chief global strategist at brokerage firm BTIG LLC , said of
recent market moves. "Half the market participants think one thing, the other half think another."
In the bond market during these months, "good news" on the economy has more consistently translated into
bad news for prices, as is normally the case. Earlier this month, the yield on the 10-year Treasury briefly rose
above 3%. In May, the yield was 1.6%. Bond yields move in the opposite direction of prices.
The bumpy road for stock investors and big swoon in bonds underscore the overreaching influence Fed
policy has had on financial markets and investors' psyche over the past several years.
Many strategists, economists and investors expect the Fed to scale back, or taper, bond purchases at its
policy meeting Tuesday and Wednesday. But some worry that the volatility of recent months will continue if
the Fed doesn't map out a clear path on further tapering.
Fed officials will meet again in October and December on monetary policy. In addition, there is the added
uncertainty among investors over who will succeed Chairman Ben Bernanke when his term expires in
January.
The question will change from "if" or "when" the Fed tapers at the coming meeting, to "how much," and that is
even more difficult for investors to answer, said Robbert van Batenburg, director of market strategy at global
brokerage Newedge. "Every piece of data will be weighed on a gold scale," he said.
The "good-news-is-bad-news" thinking has been playing out in the stock market since late May when Mr.
Bernanke first broached the possibility that the Fed could begin to pare back its unprecedented easing
efforts.
Subsequently, Mr. Bernanke and other Fed officials have stressed that the fate of its easing efforts is "data
dependent." Stronger data raise the odds of a more aggressive tightening and vice versa.
"It's policy by whim," said Mike O'Rourke , chief market strategist at JonesTrading Institutional Services . As a
result, investment "decisions are being driven by what people think the Fed thinks, rather than the actual
data."
For the stock market, that has meant an environment where the reaction to economic news can vary from day
to day.
On Aug. 23, the S&P 500 gained 0.4% after data showing that sales of new homes in July fell much more
than expected.
But on Sept. 3, the S&P 500 advanced as much as 1.1% intraday before closing up 0.4%, following data
showing manufacturing activity in August expanded at the fastest pace in more than two years.

--- Page 100 ---
Page 100 of 192 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Investors were hoping the monthly jobs report released on Sept. 6 would help cure the market's bipolar
behavior by clarifying the labor market's strength. Instead, the data added to the confusion, as it showed that
the pace of job growth may be slowing just enough to complicate the Fed's plans for paring bond purchases.
Over the long term, as economic growth becomes more consistent and the overall path of Fed policy
becomes clear, so will the market's reaction to data, investors say.
"The Fed is not the enemy," said John Stoltzfus , chief market strategist at Oppenheimer Asset Management.
He said less Fed stimulus just means the Fed is confident the economy is strengthening, and a strong
economy is good for stocks. "Good news is still good news long term," Mr. Stoltzfus said.
