Global Finance: Singapore Fund Shifts Focus --- GIC Plans to Increase Its Exposure to Stocks; Sees U.S. as Key Investment Arena
Global Finance: Singapore Fund Shifts Focus --- GIC Plans to Increase Its Exposure to Stocks; Sees
U.S. as Key Investment Arena
By P.R. Venkat
878 words
2 August 2013
The Wall Street Journal
J
C3
English
Copyright 2013 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
SINGAPORE -- Singapore sovereign-wealth fund GIC Pvt. Ltd. said it is preparing for volatile markets in the
coming year, in particular the risk of a slowdown in China, and that it will use the opportunity to boost its
exposure to stocks.
The fund that manages Singapore's foreign-exchange reserves said in its annual report for the fiscal year
ended March 31 that while the global economy is faced with challenges, GIC is more bullish on the U.S. and
sees it as a major destination for its investments.
"U.S. is the most furthest along in terms of it no longer needing policy support," Lim Chow Kiat, GIC's chief
investment officer, said in an interview. He added, "The U.S., in particular the private sector, time and again
has shown that they are able to deal with crisis and challenges. It continues to produce many companies
which are profitable and competitive."
Although GIC doesn't disclose its investment and divestment activities, the fund has been rebalancing its
portfolio since the start of the year, with a focus in the real-estate, technology and resources sectors.
In July, GIC paid $1.4 billion for MSR Resort Gold Course LLC's Hawaii, California and Arizona resorts. GIC
also bought an office building in San Francisco's financial district. The fund was part of a consortium that
purchased Houston-based BMC Software Inc . for $6.9 billion. In April, GIC was one of the partners that
bought French oil company Total SA 's regional gas pipeline network TIGF.
Among recent divestments, GIC raised about $1.3 billion by selling a portion of its stake in warehouse
operator Global Logistic Properties Ltd . in February. It raised another $250 million by selling convertible
bonds of Glencore International AG in April.
Compared with last year, GIC, which is mandated to focus on long-term investment return for a period of 20
years, lowered its cash holdings to 7% from 11% and increased its exposure to equities and fixed income
such as bonds. The fund said it has undertaken a review of its investment approach, and since April it has
started using a reference portfolio of 65% global stocks and 35% global bonds. Earlier, it was using a different
portfolio reference that varied from 60% stocks and 40% bonds to 70% stocks and 30% bonds.
While GIC says it oversees more than $100 billion in assets, analysts estimate the figure is closer to $300
billion. In addition to not disclosing investments and divestments, GIC only reports on the performance of its
portfolio over a 20-year, 10-year and five-year period. The fund reported a 4% 20-year real rate of return in
excess of global inflation for the fiscal year ended March 31. In U.S. dollar nominal terms, the fund achieved
a return of 6.5% over the same period. Its annualized nominal rates in U.S. dollar terms were 2.6% over five
years and 8.8% over 10 years.
GIC said the aim of reviewing its investment approach was to prepare it to meet a more challenging and
complex investment environment in order to earn long-term real returns.
Mr. Lim also said while China has short-term challenges, GIC has a positive long-term view of that country as
policy makers have begun to take action to address issues related to changing the mix of its economic
growth, shadow banking and dealing with excess capacities in the system.
"In the short-term as [China policy makers] do this, they will lose some growth and there is a risk that they
may lose too much of that growth," Mr. Lim said. "But we think the policy makers are fully aware of that and
they have resources to prevent excessive loss of growth."
Last month, another Singapore state-investment company, Temasek Holdings Pte. Ltd ., which manages a
portfolio of $169 billion, said it sees opportunities in the U.S. and remained committed to China. The
investment firm also is opening up an office in the U.S. to help pursue opportunities there.

--- Page 188 ---
Page 188 of 192 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
GIC, which was set up in 1981, said the shift from a "conservative asset allocation" from the earlier years to a
diversified portfolio will allow it to take full advantage of its status as a long term-oriented investor.
During the financial crisis of 2008, GIC made some high-profile investments including buying stakes in
Citigroup Inc . and UBS AG . The firm initially lost money on those stakes and still holds them; it is unclear
whether they currently have gains or losses on the investments.
According to the report, GIC's portfolio in public equities was 46% as of March 31 from 45% year ago, while
exposure to fixed income such as bonds rose to 21% from 17%. Investments in real estate and private equity
and infrastructure accounted for 26%, from 27%. In terms of geographical distribution, 44% of its portfolio is in
the U.S., up from 42% last year, while the percentage of its portfolio in Europe and Asia fell by one
percentage point each to 25% and 28%, respectively.

ARENA --- Music: Cracker Barrel: The Starbucks of Country Music
ARENA --- Music: Cracker Barrel: The Starbucks of Country Music
By Don Steinberg
1,008 words
2 August 2013
The Wall Street Journal
J
D5
English
Copyright 2013 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Stop into a Cracker Barrel restaurant, and there's a poster of Brad Paisley hanging above the cash register
and a stand-up card featuring the singer in his white hat on every table. Cracker Barrel is sponsoring Mr.
Paisley's current North American tour. In the "country store" area that every diner passes through to get in
and out of the restaurant, CDs of Mr. Paisley's latest album, "Wheelhouse" are on sale. It's a special edition
of the best-selling Sony record, released on Cracker Barrel's own music label, CB Music, with bonus songs
recorded exclusively for the restaurant chain.
Mr. Paisley jokes he has mixed feelings about becoming such a presence at a restaurant chain that fed him
through college: "I really can't go to eat there now. My face is on the menu. I feel like the Wendy's girl," he
says.
With rocking chairs on the front porch and a menu that includes the "Old Timer's Breakfast" (two eggs, grits,
gravy, biscuits, fried apples and sausage), Cracker Barrel seems like an unlikely force in the modern music
industry. But the down-home dining chain has become a serious niche player, a sort of country version of
Starbucks , selling well-targeted CDs to its restaurant customers.
"For a long time you would walk up to a counter at a Starbucks and see certain folk albums or rock albums or
whatever, and I'd think, 'Wow, they don't have us up there very much.' It's nice to have our own situation,' "
Mr. Paisley says.
"You can hyper-target your consumer there," says Brinson Strickland, a talent manager in Nashville whose
client Clint Black has an exclusive album debuting on Cracker Barrel's label next week. (Mr. Black is currently
between labels, so he had no contractual issues.) "They know exactly who their customer is. Cracker Barrel
sells exactly the same thing that country music does, which is familiarity and comfort."
Cracker Barrel, a $2.6 billion public company, has music in its roots. Founder Danny Evins was a friend of
Chet Atkins, who did early advertising spots. The current music-selling program began in 2005 with an
exclusive CD by bluegrass band Alison Krauss and Union Station (Ms. Krauss and Willie Nelson hold the
distinction of appearing on both Cracker Barrel and Starbucks CDs).
The music program has been ramping up since. Cracker Barrel has 624 restaurants in 42 states and about
four million weekly customers. Every diner passes through a knickknack shop where CDs from Cracker
Barrel's own label and others are offered, along with quilts and candles, jars of preserves, and vintage candy
brands. (Cracker Barrel cheese, owned by Kraft Foods Group, is no relation.) The company says about a
third of its diners make a retail purchase.
"We're all fighting for that space," Mr. Paisley says. "You're talking about dwindling shelf space for CDs, for
actual physical copies of your album. So any time you can put that thing in front of somebody at a store, it's a
win-win."
Music sales aren't a substantial part of Cracker Barrel's overall revenue. "It might be around 1%," says
Stephen Anderson , a restaurant analyst at Miller Tabak . That would be comparable to the percentage at
Starbucks . But there are marketing gains. In aligning with Mr. Paisley, the chain is reaching out to a younger
demographic, an effort it is also making with lower-calorie menu choices, called Wholesome Fixin's, added
this year.
"By making a special edition of Brad's album exclusively for Cracker Barrel, it incentivizes their customers to
buy the album on the spot because they can't get it anywhere else," says Gary Overton , CEO of Sony Music
Nashville.
Based in Lebanon, Tenn., near Nashville, Cracker Barrel has developed connections with labels and
management companies. There have been about 25 CB Music releases. The company isn't sharing many

--- Page 190 ---
Page 190 of 192 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
sales data, but Mr. Paisley's Sony release "Wheelhouse" sold 100,441 copies overall in its first week; Cracker
Barrel stores sold 11,721 first-week copies of its special version, or close to 12% of that total.
Last summer, a Dolly Parton CD/DVD package, "An Evening With Dolly," became Cracker Barrel's first
release to be certified gold (which for such combo packages is 50,000 copies sold). Josh Turner's "Live
Across America" released last August, has sold 92,960 copies in Cracker Barrel stores.
Next week, Mr. Black's "When I Said I Do" will be released by CB Music and sold in CD form exclusively at
Cracker Barrel stores (digital versions will be on iTunes and Amazon).
"It really takes me back to the old days when you put out a CD and the fans knew right where to go to buy it,"
Mr. Black says. Both Mr. Paisley and Mr. Black say the bonus songs on their Cracker Barrel discs are
especially suited for those fans.
"They're sort of bluegrassy and traditional, songs that otherwise are a little more traditional than what I would
do just for iTunes," Mr. Paisley says. "I think that's the future of music -- because now you can record very
cheaply. To cut a song used to cost about 10 grand. Now the technology is all digital, and you have your own
studio. So I could easily cut a few more tracks to give them, things that are a little bit niche-y."
Mr. Black and his manager appreciate the straightforward way CB Music structures deals. The company asks
for a certain number of CDs and pays the artist a fixed amount per unit. The artist is responsible for
manufacturing the discs to meet set specifications, paying musicians, and other expenses.
"It's so counterintuitive to what the rest of the music business is doing, where you need a forensic accountant
to know what you are owed in a traditional record deal," Mr. Strickland says.

Investors Get the Shaft From Gold Miners --- Shares Tumble as Price of Yellow Metal Continues to Sink and Companies Earn Less for Production...
Investors Get the Shaft From Gold Miners --- Shares Tumble as Price of Yellow Metal Continues to
Sink and Companies Earn Less for Production; 'It's Been Horrific'
By Tatyana Shumsky
812 words
2 August 2013
The Wall Street Journal
J
C4
English
Copyright 2013 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
A dividend cut at Barrick Gold Corp . is the latest setback for investors amid a brutal year for precious metals
plays.
The price of gold has plunged 22% this year, as investors anticipating an end to the Federal Reserve's loose
monetary policy sought out higher-yielding assets. But the carnage has been even worse for those seeking
to benefit from investments in companies that mine the metal.
The NYSE ArcaGold Bugs Index, which tracks 17 gold-mining companies, is down 45% in 2013. Barrick, the
world's largest gold producer, on Thursday reported an $8.56 billion second-quarter loss, compared with a
$787 million profit a year ago, and cut its dividend to five cents from 20 cents. The company's stock is off 53%
this year, falling 16 cents, or 0.9%, to $16.81, in 4 p.m. New York Stock Exchange composite trading on
Thursday.
Investors said it is a familiar turn for mining stocks, which often trailed gold prices during a 12-year bull
market that appears to have come to an end this year. Miners spent much of their windfall on acquiring new
mines, which depressed share values during the boom and are often worth significantly less today. Few
companies opted to lock in prices for gold in advance, leaving them unprotected when the market plunged
this year.
Gold's fall means miners are earning less from their production. Meanwhile, costs are coming down much
more slowly. The result: a sudden swing from profits to losses for many companies.
"If you look at the sector's trailing performance over the past few years, it's been horrific," said Mark Luschini,
who helps manage $2.9 billion with Janney Capital Management. Mr. Luschini said the
investment-management company sold its gold holdings in February and got rid of gold mining stocks a few
months ago.
In April, gold prices entered a bear market, defined as a drop of 20% or more from a recent high. On
Thursday, front-month gold for August delivery fell $1.40, or 0.1%, to $1,311 a troy ounce, off 27% from a
52-week high in October and down 31% from the record close of $1,888.70 hit in August 2011.
The decline comes as miners are struggling with acquisitions and new projects undertaken when prices were
higher. Barrick acquired copper producer Equinox Minerals Ltd . for $7.65 billion in April 2011, just after
prices for that metal had peaked. It also is facing regulatory delays and cost overruns at its Pascua-Lama
gold mining project on the border of Argentina and Chile.
Barrick Chief Executive Jamie Sokalsky said on Thursday that the company's management recently met to
review its mines with a view to cut costs. "We're making substantial changes at Barrick," he said on the
company's conference call.
Kinross Gold Corp . purchased Red Back Mining, owner of a gold mine in Mauritania, for $7.1 billion in
August 2010. It has since written down much of that acquisition price.
On Thursday, Kinross's stock ended at $5.12, down 11 cents, or 2.1%, on the NYSE. It has fallen 47% this
year. The company eliminated its dividend this week.
A Kinross spokesman said the CEO Paul Rollinson was brought in last year to address performance
problems. "He has made tough decisions and taken the company in a new direction," including cutting capital
spending by $750 million, the spokesman said.
Some investors believe gold miners' stocks have hit bottom. They said most companies have adjusted the
value of their assets to reflect lower gold prices and are making headway in cutting costs. Barrick's shares

--- Page 192 ---
Page 192 of 192 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
barely budged after it reported its loss, a sign investors already had priced the company's problems into the
value of its stock.
"Going through this crisis has given the industry religion that they can't just have a bet on gold, they have to
actually mine the gold profitably," said Vedant Mimani, lead portfolio manager at Miami-based hedge fund
Atyant Capital.
Mr. Mimani said his $85 million fund started buying gold stocks in March and has been steadily increasing its
position since then.
Still, even investors who want exposure to gold are bypassing miners. Exchange-traded funds, which let
investors trade physical gold without worrying about logistics and storage arrangements, track the metal's
price more closely than mining stocks.
SPDR Gold Trust , the world's largest gold ETF, holds about 30 million ounces of gold valued at nearly $40
billion. This is down about 30% from the 43.4 million ounces, about $72.2 billion, the fund held at the start of
the year.
